#### 2.3b Wave Transmission Mechanisms

Wave transmission is another fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It refers to the phenomenon where a wave, upon encountering a boundary, is partially or completely transmitted into the medium beyond the boundary. This section will delve into the mechanisms of wave transmission, focusing on the transmission of ocean waves at the sea surface.

##### Transmission at the Sea Surface

The sea surface, like the reflection of ocean waves, is a complex boundary that interacts with ocean waves in a variety of ways. The transmission of ocean waves at the sea surface is a critical aspect of wave interaction, as it determines the distribution of wave energy in the ocean.

The transmission of ocean waves at the sea surface can be understood in terms of the wave's incident angle and the sea surface's slope. The incident angle is the angle between the wave's direction of travel and the normal to the sea surface. The sea surface's slope is the angle between the sea surface and the horizontal.

The transmission coefficient, denoted by $t$, is a dimensionless quantity that describes the fraction of the incident wave that is transmitted at the sea surface. It is defined as the ratio of the transmitted wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. The transmission coefficient is a function of the incident angle and the sea surface's slope.

The transmission coefficient can be calculated using the Fresnel equations, which are a set of equations that describe the reflection and transmission of electromagnetic waves at an interface. The Fresnel equations are given by:

$$
t = \frac{2c_1}{c_2 + c_1}
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the two media, and $c_2$ is the wave speed in the sea surface.

The transmission coefficient is a complex quantity, and its magnitude and phase depend on the incident angle and the sea surface's slope. The magnitude of the transmission coefficient represents the fraction of the incident wave that is transmitted at the sea surface.

##### Wave Transmission and Reflection

The total wave energy at the sea surface is the sum of the reflected wave energy and the transmitted wave energy. This can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
E_{total} = E_{reflected} + E_{transmitted}
$$

where $E_{total}$ is the total wave energy, $E_{reflected}$ is the reflected wave energy, and $E_{transmitted}$ is the transmitted wave energy.

The reflection coefficient and transmission coefficient satisfy the following relationship:

$$
|r|^2 + |t|^2 = 1
$$

This relationship ensures the conservation of wave energy at the sea surface.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave absorption and its role in wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.3c Wave Reflection and Transmission Coefficients

The reflection and transmission coefficients are crucial in understanding the behavior of waves at the sea surface. These coefficients are complex quantities that describe the fraction of the incident wave that is reflected or transmitted at the sea surface. 

##### Reflection Coefficient

The reflection coefficient, denoted by $r$, is a dimensionless quantity that describes the fraction of the incident wave that is reflected at the sea surface. It is defined as the ratio of the reflected wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. The reflection coefficient is a function of the incident angle and the sea surface's slope.

The reflection coefficient can be calculated using the Fresnel equations, which are a set of equations that describe the reflection and transmission of electromagnetic waves at an interface. The Fresnel equations are given by:

$$
r = \frac{c_2 - c_1}{c_2 + c_1}
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the two media, and $c_2$ is the wave speed in the sea surface.

The reflection coefficient is a complex quantity, and its magnitude and phase depend on the incident angle and the sea surface's slope. The magnitude of the reflection coefficient represents the fraction of the incident wave that is reflected at the sea surface.

##### Transmission Coefficient

The transmission coefficient, denoted by $t$, is a dimensionless quantity that describes the fraction of the incident wave that is transmitted at the sea surface. It is defined as the ratio of the transmitted wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. The transmission coefficient is a function of the incident angle and the sea surface's slope.

The transmission coefficient can be calculated using the Fresnel equations, which are a set of equations that describe the reflection and transmission of electromagnetic waves at an interface. The Fresnel equations are given by:

$$
t = \frac{2c_1}{c_2 + c_1}
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the two media, and $c_2$ is the wave speed in the sea surface.

The transmission coefficient is a complex quantity, and its magnitude and phase depend on the incident angle and the sea surface's slope. The magnitude of the transmission coefficient represents the fraction of the incident wave that is transmitted at the sea surface.

##### Relationship between Reflection and Transmission Coefficients

The total wave energy at the sea surface is the sum of the reflected wave energy and the transmitted wave energy. This can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
|r|^2 + |t|^2 = 1
$$

This relationship ensures the conservation of wave energy at the sea surface. It also implies that the sum of the reflection and transmission coefficients is always equal to 1, regardless of the incident angle and the sea surface's slope.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave absorption and its role in wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 2.3c Reflection and Transmission Coefficients

The reflection and transmission coefficients are fundamental concepts in the study of wave interaction with boundaries. They describe the fraction of the incident wave that is reflected and transmitted at the boundary, respectively. 

##### Reflection Coefficient

The reflection coefficient, denoted by $r$, is a dimensionless quantity that describes the fraction of the incident wave that is reflected at the boundary. It is defined as the ratio of the reflected wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. The reflection coefficient is a function of the incident angle and the boundary's properties.

The reflection coefficient can be calculated using the Fresnel equations, which are a set of equations that describe the reflection and transmission of electromagnetic waves at an interface. The Fresnel equations are given by:

$$
r = \frac{c_1 - c_2}{c_1 + c_2}
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the two media, and $c_2$ is the wave speed in the boundary.

The reflection coefficient is a complex quantity, and its magnitude and phase depend on the incident angle and the boundary's properties. The magnitude of the reflection coeffic

##### Transmission Coefficient

The transmission coefficient, denoted by $t$, is a dimensionless quantity that describes the fraction of the incident wave that is transmitted at the boundary. It is defined as the ratio of the transmitted wave amplitude to the incident wave amplitude. The transmission coefficient is a function of the incident angle and the boundary's properties.

The transmission coefficient can be calculated using the Fresnel equations, which are a set of equations that describe the reflection and transmission of electromagnetic waves at an interface. The Fresnel equations are given by:

$$
t = \frac{2c_1}{c_2 + c_1}
$$

where $c_1$ and $c_2$ are the wave speeds in the two media, and $c_2$ is the wave speed in the boundary.

The transmission coefficient is a complex quantity, and its magnitude and phase depend on the incident angle and the boundary's properties. The magnitude of the transmission coefficient is always less than 1, indicating that some of the incident wave is always reflected at the boundary. The phase of the transmission coefficient can be positive or negative, depending on whether the transmitted wave is in phase or out of phase with the incident wave.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave reflection and transmission in the context of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 2.4a Definition of Wave Dispersion

Wave dispersion is a fundamental concept in the study of wave propagation. It refers to the phenomenon where waves of different wavelengths or frequencies travel at different speeds. This is in contrast to non-dispersive waves, where all waves travel at the same speed regardless of their wavelength or frequency.

In the context of ocean waves, dispersion is a critical factor that influences the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems. The dispersion of ocean waves can be understood in terms of the wave's phase speed and group speed.

##### Phase Speed

The phase speed of a wave is the speed at which a point of constant phase of the wave travels. It is given by the ratio of the wave's frequency to its wavenumber, as shown in the equation:

$$
v_{\rm p} = \frac{\omega}{k}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency and $k$ is the wavenumber. The phase speed is a function of the wave's frequency and wavelength, and it can vary significantly for different waves.

##### Group Speed

The group speed of a wave is the speed at which the wave's amplitude or energy propagates. It is given by the derivative of the wave's dispersion relation with respect to its wavenumber. The group speed is a function of the wave's frequency and wavelength, and it can also vary significantly for different waves.

The dispersion of ocean waves is typically described by the dispersion relation, which is a mathematical expression that relates the wave's frequency to its wavenumber. The dispersion relation can be linear or nonlinear, depending on the wave's properties and the medium in which it propagates.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the concept of wave dispersion, exploring its implications for wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. We will also discuss the mathematical models used to describe wave dispersion, including the linear and nonlinear wave theories.

#### 2.4b Wave Dispersion Models

Wave dispersion models are mathematical representations of the dispersion relation. These models are used to describe the propagation of waves in a medium, such as the ocean. The dispersion relation is a fundamental concept in wave theory, and it is used to calculate the phase speed and group speed of a wave.

##### Linear Wave Dispersion Models

Linear wave dispersion models are based on the assumption that the wave's amplitude is small and that the wave's properties can be described by linear equations. These models are often used for long waves in deep water, where the wave's amplitude is typically small compared to the water depth.

The dispersion relation for linear waves is given by the equation:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $k$ is the wavenumber. This equation shows that the phase speed of a linear wave is proportional to the square root of its wavenumber. This means that waves with larger wavenumbers (shorter wavelengths) travel faster than waves with smaller wavenumbers (longer wavelengths).

##### Nonlinear Wave Dispersion Models

Nonlinear wave dispersion models are used to describe waves with larger amplitudes or waves in shallow water, where the wave's properties cannot be accurately described by linear equations. These models are often used for short waves in shallow water, where the wave's amplitude can be significant compared to the water depth.

The dispersion relation for nonlinear waves is typically more complex than the dispersion relation for linear waves. It often involves higher-order terms in the wave's amplitude and wavenumber. For example, the dispersion relation for shallow water waves is given by the equation:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk + \frac{1}{2}k^2h}
$$

where $h$ is the water depth. This equation shows that the phase speed of a nonlinear wave is proportional to the square root of its wavenumber and the water depth. This means that waves with larger wavenumbers (shorter wavelengths) and larger water depths travel faster than waves with smaller wavenumbers (longer wavelengths) and smaller water depths.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of wave dispersion for the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.4c Wave Dispersion in Shallow Water

In the previous sections, we have discussed the dispersion of waves in deep water and nonlinear waves. Now, we will focus on the dispersion of waves in shallow water. Shallow water refers to a water body where the water depth is smaller than the wavelength of the wave. This condition significantly affects the wave's dispersion and propagation.

##### Shallow Water Wave Dispersion

The dispersion relation for shallow water waves is given by the equation:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk + \frac{1}{2}k^2h}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $k$ is the wavenumber, and $h$ is the water depth. This equation shows that the phase speed of a shallow water wave is proportional to the square root of its wavenumber and the water depth. This means that waves with larger wavenumbers (shorter wavelengths) and larger water depths travel faster than waves with smaller wavenumbers (longer wavelengths) and smaller water depths.

The dispersion relation for shallow water waves also shows that the phase speed increases with the square of the wavenumber. This means that waves with larger wavenumbers (shorter wavelengths) experience a larger increase in phase speed compared to waves with smaller wavenumbers (longer wavelengths). This phenomenon is known as wave steepening and is a characteristic feature of shallow water waves.

##### Wave Steepening

Wave steepening is a nonlinear effect that occurs when the wave's amplitude becomes large enough to affect the wave's dispersion. This effect is particularly pronounced in shallow water, where the wave's amplitude can significantly affect the wave's phase speed.

The wave steepening effect can be understood by considering the wave's dispersion relation. As the wave's amplitude increases, the wave's wavenumber also increases, leading to an increase in the wave's phase speed. This increase in phase speed can cause the wave's amplitude to increase even further, leading to a positive feedback loop. This feedback loop can result in the formation of steep waves, which can break and dissipate energy.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of wave dispersion and steepening for the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.4d Wave Dispersion in Deep Water

In the previous sections, we have discussed the dispersion of waves in shallow water and nonlinear waves. Now, we will focus on the dispersion of waves in deep water. Deep water refers to a water body where the water depth is larger than the wavelength of the wave. This condition significantly affects the wave's dispersion and propagation.

##### Deep Water Wave Dispersion

The dispersion relation for deep water waves is given by the equation:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $k$ is the wavenumber. This equation shows that the phase speed of a deep water wave is proportional to the square root of its wavenumber. This means that waves with larger wavenumbers (shorter wavelengths) travel faster than waves with smaller wavenumbers (longer wavelengths).

The dispersion relation for deep water waves also shows that the phase speed increases with the square root of the wavenumber. This means that waves with larger wavenumbers (shorter wavelengths) experience a larger increase in phase speed compared to waves with smaller wavenumbers (longer wavelengths). This phenomenon is known as wave steepening and is a characteristic feature of deep water waves.

##### Wave Steepening

Wave steepening is a nonlinear effect that occurs when the wave's amplitude becomes large enough to affect the wave's dispersion. This effect is particularly pronounced in deep water, where the wave's amplitude can significantly affect the wave's phase speed.

The wave steepening effect can be understood by considering the wave's dispersion relation. As the wave's amplitude increases, the wave's wavenumber also increases, leading to an increase in the wave's phase speed. This increase in phase speed can cause the wave's amplitude to increase even further, leading to a positive feedback loop. This feedback loop can result in the formation of steep waves, which can break and dissipate energy.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of wave dispersion and steepening for the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

## Chapter: Nonlinear Wave Theory

### Introduction

The study of waves is a fundamental aspect of many scientific and engineering disciplines, including but not limited to, oceanography, meteorology, and civil engineering. In the previous chapters, we have explored the linear wave theory, which is a simplified model that assumes the waves to be small and the medium to be homogeneous and isotropic. However, in many real-world scenarios, these assumptions are not always valid. This is where the nonlinear wave theory comes into play.

In this chapter, we will delve into the nonlinear wave theory, which is a more comprehensive model that can handle larger waves and non-uniform media. The nonlinear wave theory is a complex field that involves the study of wave propagation, wave interaction, and wave breaking. It is a crucial tool for understanding and predicting the behavior of waves in various physical systems.

We will begin by introducing the basic concepts of nonlinear wave theory, including the nonlinear wave equation and the concept of wave steepening. We will then explore the phenomena of wave breaking and wave interaction, which are fundamental to the understanding of wave propagation in nonlinear systems.

Next, we will discuss the methods for solving the nonlinear wave equation, including the method of characteristics and the finite difference method. These methods are essential for numerical simulations of wave propagation in nonlinear systems.

Finally, we will discuss the applications of nonlinear wave theory in various fields, including oceanography, meteorology, and civil engineering. We will also touch upon the current research trends in nonlinear wave theory and the future directions of research in this field.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive introduction to nonlinear wave theory, equipping the reader with the necessary knowledge and tools to understand and predict the behavior of waves in nonlinear systems. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in a related field, we hope that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for you.




#### 2.4b Wave Dispersion Models

Wave dispersion models are mathematical representations of the relationship between the frequency and wavenumber of a wave. These models are essential in understanding the behavior of waves in different mediums, including the ocean. In this section, we will discuss some of the most commonly used wave dispersion models.

##### The Kolsky Model

The Kolsky model, also known as the Kolsky basic model, is a mathematical model used to describe the dispersion of waves in a medium. It is based on the assumption that the wave's phase velocity is inversely proportional to its frequency. The model is given by the equation:

$$
v_{\rm p} = \frac{\omega}{k} = \frac{1}{k} \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $v_{\rm p}$ is the phase speed, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $k$ is the wavenumber, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. The Kolsky model is often used to describe the dispersion of waves in deep water, where the effects of the seafloor and the water surface are negligible.

##### The Modified Kolsky Model

The modified Kolsky model is an extension of the basic Kolsky model that takes into account the effects of the seafloor and the water surface. It is given by the equation:

$$
v_{\rm p} = \frac{\omega}{k} = \frac{1}{k} \sqrt{gk + \frac{g}{k} \left( \frac{k_0}{k} \right)^2}
$$

where $k_0$ is the wavenumber at the water surface. The modified Kolsky model is often used to describe the dispersion of waves in shallow water, where the effects of the seafloor and the water surface cannot be neglected.

##### The Multiple-Prism Dispersion Theory

The multiple-prism dispersion theory is a mathematical model used to describe the dispersion of waves in a medium with multiple interfaces. It is based on the principle of angular dispersion, which states that the phase velocity of a wave is inversely proportional to its wavelength. The theory is given by the equation:

$$
v_{\rm p} = \frac{\omega}{k} = \frac{1}{k} \sqrt{gk + \frac{g}{k} \left( \frac{k_0}{k} \right)^2 + \frac{g}{k} \left( \frac{k_1}{k} \right)^2}
$$

where $k_1$ is the wavenumber at the first interface. The multiple-prism dispersion theory is often used to describe the dispersion of waves in complex media, such as the ocean, where the effects of multiple interfaces cannot be neglected.

In the next section, we will discuss the applications of these wave dispersion models in the design of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 2.4c Dispersion in Shallow Water

Dispersion in shallow water is a complex phenomenon that is influenced by a variety of factors, including the water depth, the wavelength of the wave, and the properties of the seafloor. In this section, we will discuss the dispersion of waves in shallow water, focusing on the effects of the seafloor and the water surface.

##### The Shallow Water Equation

The shallow water equation is a simplified form of the Navier-Stokes equations that describes the motion of water in a horizontal layer. It is given by the equation:

$$
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} + c \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} + \frac{1}{f_0} \frac{\partial}{\partial x} \left( \frac{g}{f_0} \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} \right) = 0
$$

where $\eta$ is the surface elevation, $t$ is time, $x$ is the horizontal coordinate, $c$ is the phase speed of the wave, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $f_0$ is the Coriolis parameter. The shallow water equation describes the propagation of long waves in a horizontal layer of fluid, and it is often used to study the dispersion of waves in shallow water.

##### The Effects of the Seafloor

The seafloor plays a crucial role in the dispersion of waves in shallow water. The dispersion of waves is influenced by the wavelength of the wave and the properties of the seafloor. In general, the dispersion of waves increases with the wavelength of the wave and the roughness of the seafloor.

The dispersion of waves can be described by the multiple-prism dispersion theory, which is given by the equation:

$$
v_{\rm p} = \frac{\omega}{k} = \frac{1}{k} \sqrt{gk + \frac{g}{k} \left( \frac{k_0}{k} \right)^2 + \frac{g}{k} \left( \frac{k_1}{k} \right)^2}
$$

where $k_0$ is the wavenumber at the water surface and $k_1$ is the wavenumber at the seafloor. The multiple-prism dispersion theory takes into account the effects of the seafloor and the water surface, and it is often used to describe the dispersion of waves in shallow water.

##### The Effects of the Water Surface

The water surface also plays a crucial role in the dispersion of waves in shallow water. The dispersion of waves is influenced by the wavelength of the wave and the properties of the water surface. In general, the dispersion of waves increases with the wavelength of the wave and the roughness of the water surface.

The dispersion of waves can be described by the modified Kolsky model, which is given by the equation:

$$
v_{\rm p} = \frac{\omega}{k} = \frac{1}{k} \sqrt{gk + \frac{g}{k} \left( \frac{k_0}{k} \right)^2}
$$

where $k_0$ is the wavenumber at the water surface. The modified Kolsky model takes into account the effects of the water surface, and it is often used to describe the dispersion of waves in shallow water.

In the next section, we will discuss the dispersion of waves in deep water, focusing on the effects of the water column and the properties of the seafloor.




#### 2.4c Effects of Dispersion on Wave Propagation

Wave dispersion is a phenomenon that affects the propagation of waves in a medium. It is the dependence of the phase velocity of a wave on its frequency. In the previous section, we discussed some of the commonly used wave dispersion models, including the Kolsky model and the multiple-prism dispersion theory. In this section, we will explore the effects of dispersion on wave propagation.

##### Dispersion and Wave Speed

As we have seen in the previous section, the phase velocity of a wave is inversely proportional to its frequency in the Kolsky model. This means that higher frequency waves will travel faster than lower frequency waves. This is a key characteristic of dispersion and is what distinguishes it from non-dispersive media, where the phase velocity is independent of frequency.

In the multiple-prism dispersion theory, the phase velocity is also inversely proportional to the frequency, but with an additional term that accounts for the effects of multiple interfaces. This term can lead to more complex dispersion patterns, depending on the properties of the interfaces.

##### Dispersion and Wave Energy

Dispersion also affects the distribution of wave energy in a medium. In a dispersive medium, different frequency components of a wave will travel at different speeds. This can lead to the spreading of the wave energy over time, a phenomenon known as wave spreading.

In the context of ocean waves, this can have significant implications for the energy transfer between different parts of the ocean. For example, in the North Atlantic, the dispersion of ocean waves can lead to the transfer of energy from the deep ocean to the surface, which can affect the ocean's mixing and heat transport properties.

##### Dispersion and Wave Interactions

Dispersion can also affect the interactions between waves. In a dispersive medium, different frequency components of a wave can interact with each other and with waves of different frequencies. This can lead to phenomena such as wave steepening and wave breaking, which can have significant implications for the propagation of waves in the ocean.

In the context of offshore energy systems, understanding the effects of dispersion on wave interactions is crucial for predicting the behavior of waves around these systems. This can help in the design of more efficient and robust energy systems.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical models that describe wave dispersion and how these models can be used to predict the effects of dispersion on wave propagation.

In this chapter, we will delve into the study of plane progressive regular waves, a fundamental aspect of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. These waves are characterized by their uniformity and predictability, making them an essential component in the design and operation of various marine structures.

We will begin by defining what plane progressive regular waves are and how they differ from other types of waves. We will then explore the mathematical models that describe these waves, including the wave equation and the dispersion relation. These models will be presented in the popular Markdown format, using the $ and $$ delimiters to insert math expressions in TeX and LaTeX style syntax. This content is then rendered using the highly popular MathJax library.

#### 3.1a Characteristics of Regular Waves

Regular waves, also known as progressive waves, are a type of wave that propagate through a medium without changing their shape. They are characterized by their uniformity and predictability, making them an essential component in the design and operation of various marine structures.

#### Wavelength and Frequency

The wavelength ($\lambda$) and frequency ($f$) are two fundamental properties of regular waves. The wavelength is the distance between successive peaks or troughs of the wave, while the frequency is the number of cycles that pass a given point in one second. These properties are related by the equation:

$$
\lambda = \frac{c}{f}
$$

where $c$ is the speed of the wave.

#### Amplitude

The amplitude of a wave is the maximum displacement of the wave from its equilibrium position. It is a measure of the energy of the wave. The amplitude of a regular wave is typically small compared to the wavelength, and it is often assumed to be constant across the wave.

#### Dispersion

Dispersion is the phenomenon where waves of different wavelengths travel at different speeds. In the case of regular waves, the speed of the wave is dependent on its wavelength. This can be represented by the dispersion relation:

$$
c = c(f)
$$

where $c$ is the speed of the wave and $f$ is the frequency.

#### Water Depth and Wave Steepness

The properties of regular waves are influenced by the water depth and the wave steepness. The wave steepness, defined as the ratio of the wave height to the wavelength, affects the wave's ability to propagate. In deep water, where the water depth is greater than half the wavelength, the wave steepness is small, and the wave can propagate without significant energy loss. However, in shallow water, where the water depth is less than half the wavelength, the wave steepness is large, and the wave can break, leading to energy dissipation.

#### Practical Applications

The study of regular waves is crucial in the field of ocean engineering. Regular waves are used in the design of ships and offshore energy systems. They are also used in wave prediction and mitigation, particularly in the design of coastal structures and harbors.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical models that describe regular waves, including the wave equation and the dispersion relation.

#### 3.1b Regular Wave Propagation

Regular waves propagate through a medium, carrying energy and momentum. The propagation of regular waves is governed by the wave equation, which describes how the wave's properties change over time and space.

#### Wave Equation

The wave equation for regular waves in deep water is given by:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial x^2}
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave elevation, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, and $c$ is the speed of the wave. This equation describes how the wave elevation changes over time and space, and it is a fundamental tool in the study of regular waves.

#### Wave Propagation in Deep Water

In deep water, where the water depth is greater than half the wavelength, the wave propagation is primarily in the horizontal direction. The wave's energy is primarily in the horizontal direction, and the wave's amplitude decreases with depth. This is due to the wave's interaction with the bottom boundary, which causes energy dissipation.

#### Wave Propagation in Shallow Water

In shallow water, where the water depth is less than half the wavelength, the wave propagation is more complex. The wave's energy is distributed in both the horizontal and vertical directions, and the wave's amplitude can increase with depth. This is due to the wave's interaction with the bottom boundary, which can cause wave reflection and refraction.

#### Wave Propagation in Shallow Water with a Sloping Bottom

In shallow water with a sloping bottom, the wave propagation can be even more complex. The wave's energy can be reflected and refracted by the bottom boundary, leading to wave energy dissipation and redistribution. This can be described by the wave equation with additional terms representing the wave's interaction with the bottom boundary.
