#### 3.1b Regular Wave Equations

The study of regular waves involves understanding the equations that govern their propagation. These equations are derived from the fundamental principles of fluid dynamics and wave theory. In this section, we will discuss the regular wave equations and their implications for wave propagation.

#### The Wave Equation

The wave equation is a second-order linear partial differential equation that describes the propagation of a variety of waves, such as sound waves, light waves, and water waves. For regular waves, the wave equation can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial t^2} = c^2 \frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial x^2}
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave elevation, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, and $c$ is the wave speed.

#### The Dispersion Relation

The dispersion relation is a mathematical expression that relates the wave speed to the wave frequency and wavelength. For regular waves, the dispersion relation can be written as:

$$
c = \sqrt{\frac{g \lambda}{2\pi}}
$$

where $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\lambda$ is the wavelength, and $\pi$ is the ratio of the circumference of a circle to its diameter.

#### The Wave Steepness

The wave steepness, defined as the ratio of the wave height to the wavelength, is a crucial parameter in the study of regular waves. It affects the wave's ability to propagate and can lead to wave breaking in shallow water. The wave steepness can be calculated using the equation:

$$
S = \frac{H}{\lambda}
$$

where $H$ is the wave height and $\lambda$ is the wavelength.

#### The Shallow Water Approximation

In shallow water, where the water depth is less than half the wavelength, the wave steepness is large, and the wave can break. This can be approximated by the shallow water approximation, which assumes that the wave speed is equal to the square root of the product of the acceleration due to gravity and the water depth. This approximation can be written as:

$$
c \approx \sqrt{g h}
$$

where $h$ is the water depth.

#### The Deep Water Approximation

In deep water, where the water depth is greater than half the wavelength, the wave steepness is small, and the wave can propagate without significant energy loss. This can be approximated by the deep water approximation, which assumes that the wave speed is equal to the square root of the product of the acceleration due to gravity and the wavelength. This approximation can be written as:

$$
c \approx \sqrt{g \lambda}
$$

These equations provide a mathematical framework for understanding the propagation of regular waves. They are fundamental to the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 3.1c Applications of Regular Waves

Regular waves have a wide range of applications in various fields, including but not limited to, oceanography, civil engineering, and offshore energy systems. In this section, we will discuss some of these applications in more detail.

#### Oceanography

In oceanography, regular waves are used to study ocean currents, wave propagation, and wave breaking. The wave steepness, as discussed in the previous section, is a crucial parameter in these studies. For instance, the wave steepness can be used to predict wave breaking, which is a significant factor in the mixing of the ocean's surface waters.

#### Civil Engineering

In civil engineering, regular waves are used in the design and analysis of structures such as bridges, dams, and coastal defenses. The wave steepness is particularly important in these applications, as it affects the wave's ability to propagate and can lead to wave breaking, which can cause structural damage.

#### Offshore Energy Systems

In the field of offshore energy systems, regular waves are used in the design and analysis of offshore structures such as oil rigs and wave energy converters. The wave steepness is a critical parameter in these applications, as it affects the wave's ability to propagate and can lead to wave breaking, which can cause structural damage.

#### Wave Energy Converters

Wave energy converters (WECs) are devices that convert the kinetic energy of waves into electrical energy. The design and analysis of WECs require a deep understanding of regular waves, including their propagation, steepness, and breaking.

#### Wave Breaking

Wave breaking is a complex phenomenon that is still not fully understood. However, it is known that wave breaking is more likely to occur when the wave steepness is large, which is often the case in shallow water. The study of wave breaking is crucial for many applications, including the design of offshore structures and the prediction of ocean currents.

In conclusion, regular waves play a crucial role in many fields, and their study is essential for understanding and predicting various phenomena. The wave steepness, in particular, is a key parameter in these applications, and its understanding is crucial for the successful application of regular waves in these fields.




#### 3.2a Definition of Wave Height and Period

Wave height and period are fundamental parameters in the study of ocean waves. They provide crucial information about the wave's energy, propagation, and potential for breaking.

#### Wave Height

The wave height, denoted as "H", is the vertical distance between the crest (highest point) and the trough (lowest point) of a wave. It is a measure of the wave's amplitude and is directly related to the wave's energy. The larger the wave height, the more energy the wave carries.

In fluid dynamics, the wave height is defined as the difference between the elevations of a crest and a neighboring trough. This definition is used by mariners, as well as in coastal, ocean, and naval engineering.

In the context of regular waves, the wave height can be defined in different ways depending on the context. For instance, the significant wave height ("H"<sub>s</sub>) is defined as the mean wave height of the highest third of the waves. It is usually defined as four times the standard deviation of the surface elevation, or equivalently as four times the square root of the zeroth-order moment (area) of the wave spectrum. The symbol "H"<sub>m0</sub> is used for this latter definition.

Another wave-height statistic in common usage is the root-mean-square (or RMS) wave height "H"<sub>rms</sub>, defined as:

$$
H_\text{rms} = \sqrt{ \frac{1}{N} \sum_{m=1}^N H_m^2},
$$

with "H"<sub>m</sub> again denoting the individual wave heights in a certain time series.

#### Wave Period

The wave period, denoted as "T", is the time it takes for a wave to complete one full oscillation. It is a measure of the wave's frequency and is inversely related to the wave's wavelength. The longer the period, the lower the frequency and the larger the wavelength.

The wave period is a crucial parameter in the study of ocean waves. It affects the wave's speed, energy, and potential for breaking. The wave period can be calculated from the wave height and wavelength using the formula:

$$
T = \frac{\lambda}{c},
$$

where "λ" is the wavelength and "c" is the wave speed.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the concepts of wave height and period, exploring their implications for wave propagation, energy transfer, and wave breaking.

#### 3.2b Wave Height and Period Measurement Techniques

The measurement of wave height and period is a critical aspect of ocean wave research. It allows us to understand the characteristics of waves, their propagation, and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. There are several techniques available for measuring wave height and period, each with its own advantages and limitations.

#### Direct Measurement Techniques

Direct measurement techniques involve physically measuring the wave height and period. This can be done using a variety of instruments, including wave gauges, wave riders, and wave radar.

Wave gauges, such as the Acoustic Wave Rider (AWR) and the Electromagnetic Wave Rider (EMWR), are instruments that are deployed in the water column to directly measure wave height and period. The AWR, for instance, uses an acoustic signal to measure the vertical displacement of the water surface, while the EMWR uses electromagnetic signals.

Wave riders, on the other hand, are instruments that are placed on the surface of the water to measure wave height and period. They work by detecting the vertical displacement of the water surface as the wave passes.

Wave radar, also known as synthetic aperture radar (SAR), is a technique that uses radar signals to measure wave height and period. It works by analyzing the radar reflections from the water surface to determine the wave height and period.

#### Indirect Measurement Techniques

Indirect measurement techniques involve inferring wave height and period from other measurements. This can be done using spectral analysis, which involves analyzing the frequency content of a signal to determine the wave period.

Spectral analysis can be performed using various methods, including the least-squares spectral analysis (LSSA) and the Lomb/Scargle periodogram method. These methods allow us to estimate the wave period from a time series of wave height measurements.

Another indirect measurement technique is the use of wave models, which are mathematical models that simulate the propagation of waves in the ocean. These models can be used to estimate wave height and period based on the known characteristics of the wave source and the ocean conditions.

#### Wave Height and Period Measurement Challenges

Despite the availability of various measurement techniques, there are several challenges associated with measuring wave height and period. These include the difficulty of deploying instruments in harsh ocean conditions, the need for high-resolution data, and the complexity of wave propagation in the ocean.

Furthermore, the accuracy of wave height and period measurements is crucial for many applications, including the design of ships and offshore energy systems, the prediction of wave-induced loads, and the understanding of wave-wave interactions. Therefore, ongoing research is needed to improve the accuracy and reliability of wave height and period measurements.

#### 3.2c Wave Height and Period Prediction Models

Predicting wave height and period is a crucial aspect of ocean wave research. It allows us to forecast the behavior of waves, their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, and their impact on the marine environment. There are several models available for predicting wave height and period, each with its own assumptions and limitations.

#### Spectral Models

Spectral models are mathematical models that describe the frequency content of a wave signal. They are based on the assumption that the wave signal can be represented as a sum of sinusoidal components, each with a specific frequency and amplitude. The wave height and period can be predicted from the spectral model by integrating the spectral density over the frequency range of interest.

The spectral model can be represented as:

$$
S(\omega) = \frac{1}{2\pi} \sum_{n=1}^{N} A_n \cos(\omega t - \phi_n),
$$

where $S(\omega)$ is the spectral density, $A_n$ is the amplitude of the $n$th sinusoidal component, $\omega$ is the frequency, $t$ is time, and $\phi_n$ is the phase shift.

#### Wave Propagation Models

Wave propagation models are mathematical models that describe the propagation of waves in the ocean. They are based on the principles of fluid dynamics and take into account the effects of wind, current, and the ocean bottom on wave propagation.

The wave propagation model can be represented as:

$$
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} + c \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} + \frac{1}{2} \frac{\partial}{\partial x} \left( \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} \right)^2 + \frac{1}{f_0} \frac{\partial}{\partial x} \left( \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} \right) = 0,
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave height, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, $c$ is the wave speed, and $f_0$ is the Coriolis parameter.

#### Wave Height and Period Prediction Challenges

Despite the availability of various prediction models, there are several challenges associated with predicting wave height and period. These include the complexity of wave propagation in the ocean, the uncertainty in the input data, and the need for high-resolution predictions.

Furthermore, the accuracy of wave height and period predictions is crucial for many applications, including the design of ships and offshore energy systems, the prediction of wave-induced loads, and the understanding of wave-wave interactions. Therefore, ongoing research is needed to improve the accuracy and reliability of wave height and period predictions.




#### 3.2b Measurement of Wave Height and Period

The measurement of wave height and period is a critical aspect of oceanography and marine engineering. It allows us to understand the characteristics of ocean waves, their propagation, and their impact on ships and offshore energy systems.

#### Wave Height Measurement

The measurement of wave height is typically done using wave sensors, such as wave radar or wave buoys. These sensors can measure the wave height at different points in the ocean, providing a spatial distribution of wave heights. 

Wave radar, for instance, uses radar technology to measure the wave height. It emits a radar pulse towards the sea surface and measures the time it takes for the pulse to return. The time delay is then converted into a distance, which corresponds to the wave height.

Wave buoys, on the other hand, use a variety of sensors to measure the wave height. These sensors can include accelerometers, pressure sensors, and tilt sensors. The data from these sensors is then used to calculate the wave height.

#### Wave Period Measurement

The measurement of wave period is typically done using the same wave sensors used for wave height measurement. The wave period can be calculated from the wave height and wavelength, which can be measured directly or inferred from the wave height.

For instance, the wave period can be calculated from the wave height using the formula:

$$
T = \frac{\lambda}{c}
$$

where "T" is the wave period, "λ" is the wavelength, and "c" is the wave speed. The wavelength can be measured directly using wave sensors, or inferred from the wave height using the formula:

$$
\lambda = \frac{g}{f}
$$

where "g" is the acceleration due to gravity and "f" is the wave frequency, which can be calculated from the wave height.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave steepness and its importance in the study of ocean waves.

#### 3.2c Wave Height and Period in Regular Waves

In the context of regular waves, the wave height and period are fundamental parameters that describe the wave's energy, propagation, and potential for breaking. 

#### Wave Height in Regular Waves

In regular waves, the wave height is typically measured from the trough (lowest point) to the crest (highest point) of a single wave. This is known as the wave amplitude. The wave height can vary significantly from one wave to the next, and it is typically described by a probability distribution. 

The significant wave height ("H"<sub>s}</sub>), as mentioned earlier, is defined as the mean wave height of the highest third of the waves. It is a commonly used statistic in the study of ocean waves.

#### Wave Period in Regular Waves

The wave period in regular waves is the time it takes for a complete wave cycle to pass a fixed point. It is a measure of the wave's frequency and is inversely related to the wave's wavelength. The longer the period, the lower the frequency and the larger the wavelength.

The wave period can be calculated from the wave height and wavelength, as discussed in the previous section. It can also be calculated from the wave frequency, which is the inverse of the wave period.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave steepness and its importance in the study of ocean waves.

#### 3.2d Wave Height and Period in Irregular Waves

In contrast to regular waves, irregular waves are characterized by their unpredictability and variability. The wave height and period in irregular waves are typically described by statistical measures, as the wave height and period can vary significantly from one wave to the next.

#### Wave Height in Irregular Waves

In irregular waves, the wave height is typically described by a probability distribution. The most commonly used distribution is the Rayleigh distribution, which assumes that the wave heights are exponentially distributed. The Rayleigh distribution is given by the equation:

$$
P(H) = \frac{H}{H_s}e^{-H/H_s}
$$

where "P(H)" is the probability of a wave height "H", and "H_s" is the significant wave height.

The significant wave height in irregular waves is defined as the mean wave height of the highest third of the waves. It is a commonly used statistic in the study of ocean waves.

#### Wave Period in Irregular Waves

The wave period in irregular waves is typically described by a probability distribution. The most commonly used distribution is the exponential distribution, which assumes that the wave periods are exponentially distributed. The exponential distribution is given by the equation:

$$
P(T) = \frac{1}{\mu}e^{-T/\mu}
$$

where "P(T)" is the probability of a wave period "T", and "μ" is the mean wave period.

The mean wave period in irregular waves is defined as the average of all the wave periods. It is a commonly used statistic in the study of ocean waves.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave steepness and its importance in the study of ocean waves.




#### 3.2c Effects of Wave Height and Period on Wave Dynamics

The wave height and period are two fundamental parameters that describe the characteristics of a wave. They are interconnected and their relationship is crucial in understanding the dynamics of waves.

#### Wave Height and Period Relationship

The wave height and period are related through the wave speed, wavelength, and frequency. The wave speed is given by the formula:

$$
c = \sqrt{\frac{g\lambda}{2\pi}}
$$

where "c" is the wave speed, "g" is the acceleration due to gravity, "λ" is the wavelength, and "π" is the pi constant. The wavelength is related to the period by the formula:

$$
\lambda = cT
$$

where "T" is the period. The frequency is related to the period by the formula:

$$
f = \frac{1}{T}
$$

where "f" is the frequency.

#### Effects of Wave Height on Wave Dynamics

The wave height has a significant impact on the wave dynamics. A larger wave height indicates a higher energy level of the wave. This can lead to more significant wave effects, such as larger wave forces on structures and more pronounced wave set-up.

The wave height also affects the wave steepness, which is defined as the ratio of the wave height to the wavelength. A higher wave steepness indicates a steeper wave, which can lead to more complex wave phenomena, such as wave breaking and wave reflection.

#### Effects of Wave Period on Wave Dynamics

The wave period also plays a crucial role in wave dynamics. A longer period indicates a lower frequency, which can lead to less complex wave phenomena. However, a longer period can also result in larger wave forces on structures, due to the larger wave energy content.

The wave period also affects the wave speed and wavelength. A longer period results in a lower wave speed and a longer wavelength. This can lead to more significant wave effects, such as larger wave set-up and more pronounced wave reflection.

In conclusion, the wave height and period are two fundamental parameters that describe the characteristics of a wave. They are interconnected and their relationship is crucial in understanding the dynamics of waves. The wave height and period can have significant effects on wave dynamics, affecting wave forces, wave steepness, and wave phenomena.




#### 3.3a Definition of Wave Amplitude and Phase

The amplitude and phase of a wave are fundamental properties that describe the wave's shape and position in its cycle. 

#### Wave Amplitude

The amplitude of a wave is the maximum displacement of the wave from its mean or equilibrium position. It is a measure of the wave's strength or energy. The amplitude is directly related to the wave's energy. A larger amplitude indicates a higher energy level of the wave. 

The amplitude of a wave can be represented mathematically as:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}h
$$

where "A" is the amplitude and "h" is the wave height.

#### Wave Phase

The phase of a wave is the position of the wave in its cycle. It is measured in radians and can range from 0 to 2π. The phase is used to describe the relative position of two waves. A phase difference of 0° or 2π indicates that the waves are in phase, meaning they have the same shape and position in their cycles. A phase difference of 180° indicates that the waves are out of phase, meaning they have opposite shapes and positions in their cycles.

The phase of a wave can be represented mathematically as:

$$
\phi = \arctan\left(\frac{y}{x}\right)
$$

where "x" and "y" are the horizontal and vertical components of the wave, respectively.

#### Wave Amplitude and Phase in Regular Waves

In regular waves, the amplitude and phase are constant across the wave. This means that all points on the wave have the same amplitude and phase. This is a characteristic of plane progressive regular waves, which are the focus of this chapter.

The amplitude and phase of regular waves can be represented mathematically as:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}h
$$

and

$$
\phi = \arctan\left(\frac{y}{x}\right)
$$

where "A" is the amplitude, "h" is the wave height, "x" and "y" are the horizontal and vertical components of the wave, respectively, and "φ" is the phase.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of wave amplitude and phase on wave dynamics.

#### 3.3b Wave Amplitude and Phase in Regular Waves

In the previous section, we introduced the concepts of wave amplitude and phase. Now, we will delve deeper into these concepts and explore how they manifest in regular waves. 

#### Wave Amplitude in Regular Waves

In regular waves, the amplitude remains constant across the wave. This is a characteristic of plane progressive regular waves, which are the focus of this chapter. The amplitude of a regular wave can be represented mathematically as:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}h
$$

where "A" is the amplitude and "h" is the wave height. This equation shows that the amplitude is directly proportional to the wave height. A larger wave height indicates a larger amplitude, and thus a higher energy level of the wave.

#### Wave Phase in Regular Waves

The phase of a wave describes its position in its cycle. In regular waves, the phase is constant across the wave. This means that all points on the wave have the same phase. The phase of a regular wave can be represented mathematically as:

$$
\phi = \arctan\left(\frac{y}{x}\right)
$$

where "x" and "y" are the horizontal and vertical components of the wave, respectively. This equation shows that the phase is determined by the ratio of the vertical component to the horizontal component of the wave. A phase difference of 0° or 2π indicates that the waves are in phase, meaning they have the same shape and position in their cycles. A phase difference of 180° indicates that the waves are out of phase, meaning they have opposite shapes and positions in their cycles.

#### Wave Amplitude and Phase in Regular Waves

In regular waves, the amplitude and phase are constant across the wave. This means that all points on the wave have the same amplitude and phase. This is a characteristic of plane progressive regular waves, which are the focus of this chapter. The amplitude and phase of regular waves can be represented mathematically as:

$$
A = \frac{1}{2}h
$$

and

$$
\phi = \arctan\left(\frac{y}{x}\right)
$$

where "A" is the amplitude, "h" is the wave height, "x" and "y" are the horizontal and vertical components of the wave, respectively, and "φ" is the phase. These equations show that the amplitude and phase are determined by the wave height and the ratio of the vertical component to the horizontal component of the wave, respectively.

In the next section, we will explore how these properties of wave amplitude and phase affect the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 3.3c Effects of Wave Amplitude and Phase on Ship Motion

The motion of a ship in the ocean is significantly influenced by the wave amplitude and phase. The wave amplitude, as we have seen, is directly proportional to the wave height. A larger wave height indicates a larger amplitude, and thus a higher energy level of the wave. This energy level can cause significant motion in a ship, particularly if the ship is small or lightweight.

The phase of the wave, on the other hand, determines the timing of the wave's passage. A ship that is in phase with a wave will experience the full force of the wave, while a ship that is out of phase will experience a reduced force. This can have significant implications for the ship's stability and maneuverability.

The effects of wave amplitude and phase on ship motion can be modeled using the Taylor's series method. This method allows us to approximate the motion of a ship in a wave field by considering the first few terms of the series. The first term, known as the zero-order term, represents the mean motion of the ship. The second term, known as the first-order term, represents the wave-induced motion of the ship. The third term, known as the second-order term, represents the wave-induced motion of the ship due to the wave-induced motion of the ship.

The wave-induced motion of the ship, represented by the first-order term, is given by the equation:

$$
\eta = A \cos(\omega t - kx)
$$

where "A" is the wave amplitude, "ω" is the wave frequency, "t" is time, "k" is the wave number, and "x" is the horizontal distance from the ship. This equation shows that the wave-induced motion of the ship is a function of the wave amplitude, frequency, and number, as well as the ship's position.

The wave-induced motion of the ship due to the wave-induced motion of the ship, represented by the second-order term, is given by the equation:

$$
\eta_2 = \frac{1}{2}A^2 \cos(2\omega t - 2kx)
$$

This equation shows that the wave-induced motion of the ship due to the wave-induced motion of the ship is a function of the square of the wave amplitude, the double of the wave frequency, and the double of the wave number. This term represents the second-order wave-induced motion of the ship, which is typically smaller than the first-order wave-induced motion.

In the next section, we will explore how these effects of wave amplitude and phase on ship motion can be mitigated using various techniques, such as wave-induced motion compensation and wave-induced motion prediction.




#### 3.3b Measurement of Wave Amplitude and Phase

The measurement of wave amplitude and phase is a crucial aspect of oceanography and marine engineering. It allows us to understand the behavior of waves and their interaction with various structures and systems. In this section, we will discuss the methods used to measure wave amplitude and phase.

#### Wave Amplitude Measurement

The measurement of wave amplitude is typically done using wave gauges. These devices are designed to measure the vertical displacement of the water surface due to the passage of a wave. The wave height, and hence the wave amplitude, can then be calculated from the measured displacement.

Wave gauges can be of various types, including mechanical, electrical, and optical types. Mechanical wave gauges, such as the oscillating water column (OWC) wave gauge, use a pendulum to measure the vertical displacement of the water surface. Electrical wave gauges, such as the electromagnetic wave gauge, use an electromagnetic field to measure the displacement. Optical wave gauges, such as the laser wave gauge, use laser light to measure the displacement.

#### Wave Phase Measurement

The measurement of wave phase is typically done using phase meters. These devices are designed to measure the phase difference between two waves. The phase difference can then be used to determine the relative position of the waves.

Phase meters can be of various types, including optical, acoustic, and radar types. Optical phase meters, such as the interferometric scattering microscopy (iSCAT) phase meter, use light scattering to measure the phase difference. Acoustic phase meters, such as the acoustic phase meter, use sound waves to measure the phase difference. Radar phase meters, such as the radar phase meter, use radar waves to measure the phase difference.

#### Least-Squares Spectral Analysis (LSSA)

The Least-Squares Spectral Analysis (LSSA) is a method used to estimate the spectral power of a signal. It involves computing the least-squares spectrum by performing the least-squares approximation multiple times, each time for a different frequency.

The LSSA can be implemented in a few lines of MATLAB code. For each frequency in a desired set of frequencies, sine and cosine functions are evaluated at the times corresponding to the data samples. Dot products of the data vector with the sinusoid vectors are taken and appropriately normalized. A time shift is calculated for each frequency to orthogonalize the sine and cosine components before the dot product. Finally, a power is computed from those two amplitude components.

This method treats each sinusoidal component independently, even though they may not be orthogonal to data points. It is also possible to perform a full simultaneous or in-context least-squares fit by solving a matrix equation and partitioning the total data variance between the specified sinusoid frequencies. Such a matrix least-squares solution is natively available in MATLAB as the backslash operator.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of wave amplitude and phase on wave dynamics.

#### 3.3c Wave Amplitude and Phase in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave amplitude and phase behavior can be significantly different from that in shallow water. This is due to the effects of water depth on wave propagation. In this section, we will discuss the wave amplitude and phase in deep water.

#### Wave Amplitude in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave amplitude can be significantly larger than in shallow water. This is due to the fact that in deep water, the wave can propagate without significant energy loss due to bottom friction. The wave amplitude in deep water can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
A = \frac{H}{2} \sqrt{\frac{gT}{2\pi}}
$$

where $A$ is the wave amplitude, $H$ is the water depth, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $T$ is the wave period.

#### Wave Phase in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave phase can also be significantly different from that in shallow water. This is due to the fact that in deep water, the wave can propagate without significant phase shift due to bottom friction. The wave phase in deep water can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\phi = \frac{2\pi}{\lambda} \int_{0}^{L} \frac{H}{H + y} dy
$$

where $\phi$ is the wave phase, $\lambda$ is the wave length, $L$ is the wave length, and $y$ is the vertical coordinate (positive upwards).

#### Wave Amplitude and Phase in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave amplitude and phase can be significantly larger and different from that in shallow water. This is due to the effects of water depth on wave propagation. The wave amplitude and phase in deep water can be calculated using the equations discussed above.

In the next section, we will discuss the wave amplitude and phase in shallow water.




#### 3.3c Effects of Wave Amplitude and Phase on Wave Dynamics

The amplitude and phase of a wave play a crucial role in its dynamics. The amplitude of a wave is directly related to its energy, and the phase of a wave determines its position in space and time. In this section, we will discuss the effects of wave amplitude and phase on wave dynamics.

#### Wave Amplitude and Energy

The amplitude of a wave is directly proportional to its energy. This relationship is described by the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2
$$

where $E$ is the energy, $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $A$ is the amplitude of the wave. This equation shows that the energy of a wave is proportional to the square of its amplitude. Therefore, a wave with a larger amplitude has more energy than a wave with a smaller amplitude.

The energy of a wave is also related to its power, which is the rate at which the wave transfers energy. The power of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
P = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2 \omega
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave. This equation shows that the power of a wave is proportional to the square of its amplitude and its angular frequency. Therefore, a wave with a larger amplitude or a higher angular frequency has more power.

#### Wave Phase and Position

The phase of a wave determines its position in space and time. The phase of a wave is defined as the angle between the wave and a reference line. The phase of a wave can be determined by measuring the time it takes for the wave to pass a fixed point.

The phase of a wave is also related to its velocity. The velocity of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
v = \omega / k
$$

where $k$ is the wave number. This equation shows that the velocity of a wave is inversely proportional to its wave number. Therefore, a wave with a larger wave number has a higher velocity.

#### Wave Amplitude and Phase in Plane Progressive Regular Waves

In plane progressive regular waves, the wave amplitude and phase are constant across the wavefront. This means that the wave amplitude and phase are not affected by the direction of propagation. However, the wave amplitude and phase can be affected by the wave steepness and the wave number.

The wave steepness, defined as the ratio of the wave amplitude to the wavelength, can affect the wave amplitude and phase. A larger wave steepness can result in a larger wave amplitude and a higher wave phase.

The wave number, defined as the number of waves per unit length, can also affect the wave amplitude and phase. A larger wave number can result in a higher wave amplitude and a faster wave phase.

In conclusion, the amplitude and phase of a wave play a crucial role in its dynamics. The amplitude of a wave is directly related to its energy, and the phase of a wave determines its position in space and time. In plane progressive regular waves, the wave amplitude and phase are constant across the wavefront, but they can be affected by the wave steepness and the wave number.




#### 3.4a Definition of Wave Velocity

Wave velocity is a fundamental concept in the study of waves. It is defined as the speed at which a wave propagates through a medium. In the context of ocean waves, the medium is the water, and the wave velocity is the speed at which the wave travels across the surface of the water.

The wave velocity is a crucial parameter in understanding the behavior of waves. It is directly related to the wave's frequency and wavelength, as shown by the equation:

$$
v = \frac{\omega}{k}
$$

where $v$ is the wave velocity, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, and $k$ is the wave number. This equation shows that the wave velocity is inversely proportional to the wave number. Therefore, a wave with a larger wave number has a higher velocity.

The wave velocity is also related to the wave's energy and power. As discussed in the previous section, the energy and power of a wave are proportional to the square of its amplitude and its angular frequency. Therefore, a wave with a larger amplitude or a higher angular frequency has more energy and power.

In the context of ocean waves, the wave velocity is particularly important due to the complex interaction between the waves and the ocean. The wave velocity can change depending on the depth of the water, the presence of currents, and the characteristics of the seafloor. Understanding the wave velocity is crucial for predicting the behavior of ocean waves and their impact on ships and offshore energy systems.

In the next section, we will discuss the different types of wave velocities and their significance in the study of ocean waves.

#### 3.4b Calculation of Wave Velocity

The calculation of wave velocity is a crucial step in understanding the behavior of waves. As we have seen, the wave velocity is directly related to the wave's frequency and wavelength. Therefore, by knowing the frequency and wavelength of a wave, we can calculate its velocity.

The frequency of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
\omega = 2\pi f
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency, and $f$ is the frequency. The frequency of a wave is the number of complete oscillations it makes in one second.

The wavelength of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
\lambda = \frac{v}{\omega}
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavelength, and $v$ is the wave velocity. The wavelength of a wave is the distance between successive peaks or troughs of the wave.

Substituting the equation for frequency into the equation for wavelength, we get:

$$
\lambda = \frac{v}{2\pi f}
$$

Solving this equation for $v$, we get the equation for wave velocity:

$$
v = \frac{\omega}{k}
$$

This equation shows that the wave velocity is inversely proportional to the wave number. Therefore, a wave with a larger wave number has a higher velocity.

In the context of ocean waves, the wave velocity can change depending on the depth of the water, the presence of currents, and the characteristics of the seafloor. Therefore, the calculation of wave velocity is a complex task that requires a deep understanding of the physical properties of the ocean.

In the next section, we will discuss the different types of wave velocities and their significance in the study of ocean waves.

#### 3.4c Wave Velocity in Different Media

The wave velocity is a fundamental concept in the study of waves. It is defined as the speed at which a wave propagates through a medium. In the context of ocean waves, the medium is the water. However, the wave velocity can vary significantly depending on the properties of the medium. In this section, we will discuss the wave velocity in different media, focusing on the effects of water depth and currents.

##### Water Depth

The wave velocity in water is influenced by the depth of the water. As the depth of the water increases, the wave velocity decreases. This is due to the fact that the wave energy is spread over a larger volume of water as the depth increases. This phenomenon is described by the equation:

$$
v = \frac{\omega}{k} \sqrt{\frac{g}{k}}
$$

where $v$ is the wave velocity, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $k$ is the wave number, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. This equation shows that the wave velocity is proportional to the square root of the wave number and the acceleration due to gravity. Therefore, a wave with a larger wave number or a larger acceleration due to gravity has a higher wave velocity.

##### Currents

Currents can also affect the wave velocity. If a wave is propagating in a direction that is not parallel to the current, the wave velocity can be altered. This is due to the fact that the wave energy can be transferred to the current, leading to a decrease in the wave velocity. The effect of currents on wave velocity is described by the equation:

$$
v = \frac{\omega}{k} \sqrt{\frac{g}{k} - \frac{g}{c}}
$$

where $c$ is the speed of the current. This equation shows that the wave velocity is proportional to the square root of the wave number and the acceleration due to gravity minus the acceleration due to the current. Therefore, a wave with a larger wave number or a larger acceleration due to gravity relative to the acceleration due to the current has a higher wave velocity.

In the next section, we will discuss the wave velocity in different types of waves, focusing on the effects of wave steepness and wave age.




#### 3.4b Calculation of Wave Velocity

The calculation of wave velocity is a crucial step in understanding the behavior of waves. As we have seen, the wave velocity is directly related to the wave's frequency and wavelength. Therefore, by knowing the frequency and wavelength of a wave, we can calculate its velocity.

The frequency of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
f = \frac{1}{T}
$$

where $f$ is the frequency, and $T$ is the period of the wave. The period is the time it takes for one complete wave to pass a given point.

The wavelength of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
\lambda = cT
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavelength, and $c$ is the wave speed. The wave speed is the speed at which the wave propagates through the medium.

Substituting the equation for the period into the equation for the wavelength, we get:

$$
\lambda = \frac{c}{f}
$$

Rearranging this equation, we get the equation for the wave velocity:

$$
v = \frac{\lambda}{T} = \frac{c}{f}
$$

This equation shows that the wave velocity is inversely proportional to the frequency. Therefore, a wave with a higher frequency has a lower velocity, and a wave with a lower frequency has a higher velocity.

In the context of ocean waves, the wave velocity can be affected by various factors, including the depth of the water, the presence of currents, and the characteristics of the seafloor. For example, in deep water, the wave velocity is given by the equation:

$$
v = \sqrt{\frac{g\lambda}{2\pi}}
$$

where $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity. This equation shows that the wave velocity increases with the wavelength. Therefore, larger waves have higher velocities.

In the next section, we will discuss the different types of wave velocities and their significance in the study of ocean waves.

#### 3.4c Wave Velocity Measurement Techniques

The measurement of wave velocity is a crucial aspect of understanding the behavior of waves. There are several techniques available for measuring wave velocity, each with its own advantages and limitations. In this section, we will discuss some of the most commonly used techniques.

##### Direct Measurement Techniques

Direct measurement techniques involve directly measuring the wave velocity. One such technique is the use of a wave meter, a device that measures the frequency and wavelength of a wave. By knowing the frequency and wavelength, the wave velocity can be calculated using the equation:

$$
v = \frac{\lambda}{T} = \frac{c}{f}
$$

Another direct measurement technique is the use of a Doppler radar, which can measure the velocity of a wave by detecting the Doppler shift of the wave's frequency.

##### Indirect Measurement Techniques

Indirect measurement techniques involve inferring the wave velocity from other measurements. One such technique is the use of a wave rider, a device that measures the displacement of a wave. By knowing the displacement and the wave's frequency, the wave velocity can be calculated using the equation:

$$
v = \frac{\lambda}{T} = \frac{c}{f}
$$

Another indirect measurement technique is the use of a wave gauge, a device that measures the surface elevation of a wave. By knowing the surface elevation and the wave's frequency, the wave velocity can be calculated using the equation:

$$
v = \frac{\lambda}{T} = \frac{c}{f}
$$

##### Remote Sensing Techniques

Remote sensing techniques involve measuring the wave velocity from a distance. One such technique is the use of a radar, which can measure the velocity of a wave by detecting the radar reflection from the wave's surface. Another remote sensing technique is the use of a satellite, which can measure the wave velocity by detecting the satellite reflection from the wave's surface.

In the next section, we will discuss the different types of wave velocities and their significance in the study of ocean waves.

#### 3.4d Wave Velocity in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave velocity is primarily influenced by the wavelength and the frequency of the wave. The wave velocity in deep water can be calculated using the equation:

$$
v = \sqrt{\frac{g\lambda}{2\pi}}
$$

where $v$ is the wave velocity, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\lambda$ is the wavelength, and $\pi$ is the pi constant. This equation is known as the deep-water wave velocity equation.

The deep-water wave velocity equation shows that the wave velocity increases with the wavelength. This means that larger waves have higher velocities. Additionally, the wave velocity decreases with the frequency. This means that waves with higher frequencies have lower velocities.

In deep water, the wave velocity can also be affected by the presence of currents. If there is a current flowing in the same direction as the wave, the wave velocity can increase. If there is a current flowing in the opposite direction of the wave, the wave velocity can decrease.

The wave velocity in deep water is also influenced by the characteristics of the seafloor. For example, if the seafloor is rough, it can cause the wave to break, which can decrease the wave velocity. If the seafloor is smooth, it can cause the wave to propagate more smoothly, which can increase the wave velocity.

In the next section, we will discuss the wave velocity in shallow water and how it differs from the wave velocity in deep water.

#### 3.4e Wave Velocity in Shallow Water

In shallow water, the wave velocity is primarily influenced by the water depth and the wave's steepness. The wave velocity in shallow water can be calculated using the equation:

$$
v = \sqrt{\frac{g\lambda}{2\pi}\tanh\left(\frac{2\pi h}{\lambda}\right)}
$$

where $v$ is the wave velocity, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\lambda$ is the wavelength, $\pi$ is the pi constant, and $h$ is the water depth. This equation is known as the shallow-water wave velocity equation.

The shallow-water wave velocity equation shows that the wave velocity decreases with the water depth. This means that waves in shallow water have lower velocities compared to waves in deep water. Additionally, the wave velocity increases with the wave's steepness. This means that steeper waves have higher velocities.

In shallow water, the wave velocity can also be affected by the presence of currents. If there is a current flowing in the same direction as the wave, the wave velocity can increase. If there is a current flowing in the opposite direction of the wave, the wave velocity can decrease.

The wave velocity in shallow water is also influenced by the characteristics of the seafloor. For example, if the seafloor is rough, it can cause the wave to break, which can decrease the wave velocity. If the seafloor is smooth, it can cause the wave to propagate more smoothly, which can increase the wave velocity.

In the next section, we will discuss the wave velocity in deep water and how it differs from the wave velocity in shallow water.

#### 3.4f Wave Velocity in Harbor

In a harbor, the wave velocity is primarily influenced by the harbor's geometry and the wave's incident angle. The wave velocity in a harbor can be calculated using the equation:

$$
v = \sqrt{\frac{g\lambda}{2\pi}\tanh\left(\frac{2\pi h}{\lambda}\right)}
$$

where $v$ is the wave velocity, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\lambda$ is the wavelength, $\pi$ is the pi constant, and $h$ is the water depth. This equation is known as the harbor wave velocity equation.

The harbor wave velocity equation shows that the wave velocity decreases with the water depth. This means that waves in a harbor have lower velocities compared to waves in deep water. Additionally, the wave velocity increases with the wave's steepness. This means that steeper waves have higher velocities.

In a harbor, the wave velocity can also be affected by the presence of currents. If there is a current flowing in the same direction as the wave, the wave velocity can increase. If there is a current flowing in the opposite direction of the wave, the wave velocity can decrease.

The wave velocity in a harbor is also influenced by the characteristics of the harbor's walls. For example, if the walls are rough, it can cause the wave to break, which can decrease the wave velocity. If the walls are smooth, it can cause the wave to propagate more smoothly, which can increase the wave velocity.

In the next section, we will discuss the wave velocity in deep water and how it differs from the wave velocity in a harbor.

#### 3.4c Wave Velocity Measurement Techniques

The measurement of wave velocity is a crucial aspect of understanding the behavior of waves. There are several techniques available for measuring wave velocity, each with its own advantages and limitations. In this section, we will discuss some of the most commonly used techniques for measuring wave velocity.

##### Doppler Velocity Log (DVL)

The Doppler Velocity Log (DVL) is a common technique used for measuring wave velocity. It works by emitting a series of acoustic pulses into the water column. The pulses are then reflected off the wave surfaces and returned to the DVL. By analyzing the Doppler shift of the reflected pulses, the wave velocity can be determined.

The DVL is particularly useful for measuring wave velocity in deep water, where other techniques may not be as effective. It is also capable of measuring wave velocity in both the horizontal and vertical directions, providing a more comprehensive understanding of wave dynamics.

##### Acoustic Doppler Current Profiler (ADCP)

The Acoustic Doppler Current Profiler (ADCP) is another commonly used technique for measuring wave velocity. It works by emitting a series of acoustic pulses into the water column, similar to the DVL. However, the ADCP also measures the Doppler shift of the reflected pulses in three dimensions, providing information about the velocity of the water in all directions.

The ADCP is particularly useful for measuring wave velocity in complex environments, such as in the presence of strong currents or near offshore structures. It can also provide information about the wave direction and the water's surface roughness, which can be useful for understanding wave dynamics.

##### Wave Rider Buoys

Wave rider buoys are another common technique for measuring wave velocity. They work by measuring the motion of the buoy due to the passing waves. By analyzing the motion of the buoy, the wave velocity can be determined.

Wave rider buoys are particularly useful for measuring wave velocity in shallow water, where other techniques may not be as effective. They can also provide information about the wave direction and the water's surface roughness, which can be useful for understanding wave dynamics.

##### Wave Radar

Wave radar is a relatively new technique for measuring wave velocity. It works by emitting a series of radar pulses into the air above the water surface. The pulses are then reflected off the wave surfaces and returned to the radar. By analyzing the time delay of the reflected pulses, the wave velocity can be determined.

Wave radar is particularly useful for measuring wave velocity in high wind conditions, where other techniques may not be as effective. It can also provide information about the wave direction and the water's surface roughness, which can be useful for understanding wave dynamics.

In the next section, we will discuss the different types of wave velocities and their significance in the study of ocean waves.

The study of wave energy density and flux is essential for various applications, including the design and analysis of ships, offshore structures, and renewable energy systems. Understanding these parameters allows us to predict the forces exerted by waves on these systems, which is crucial for their design and operation. 

We will begin by defining wave energy density and flux, and discussing their physical interpretations. We will then explore the mathematical expressions that describe these parameters, using the popular Markdown format and the MathJax library for rendering mathematical expressions. ### Subsection: 4.1a Energy Conservation Equations for Waves

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of wave energy density and flux, and discussed their importance in the study of ocean waves. In this section, we will delve deeper into the mathematical description of these parameters, and explore the energy conservation equations for waves.

The energy conservation equation for waves is a fundamental principle that describes the balance of energy in a wave field. It is derived from the general equation of heat transfer, which can be written as:

$$
\rho {\partial k\over{\partial t}} = -\rho {\bf v}\cdot\nabla k - \rho {\bf v}\cdot\nabla h + \rho T{\bf v}\cdot \nabla s + \nabla\cdot(\sigma\cdot {\bf v}) - \sigma_{ij}{\partial v_{i}\over{\partial x_{j}}}
$$

where $\rho$ is the density, $k$ is the kinetic energy, $h$ is the enthalpy, $T$ is the temperature, $s$ is the entropy, $\sigma$ is the stress tensor, and $v_i$ and $v_j$ are the components of the velocity vector.

The energy conservation equation can be further simplified by considering the case where thermal conduction and viscous forces are absent. In this case, the equation for entropy production collapses to $Ds/Dt=0$, showing that ideal fluid flow is isentropic.

The energy conservation equation for waves can be written as:

$$
\rho {\partial \eta\over{\partial t}} = -\rho {\bf v}\cdot\nabla \eta + \rho T{\bf v}\cdot \nabla s + \nabla\cdot(\sigma\cdot {\bf v}) - \sigma_{ij}{\partial v_{i}\over{\partial x_{j}}}
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, and $s$ is the wave entropy.

This equation describes the balance of energy in a wave field, and is a key tool in the study of wave energy density and flux. It allows us to predict the forces exerted by waves on ships and offshore energy systems, and to optimize the design of these systems for maximum efficiency and safety.

In the next section, we will discuss the methods for measuring wave energy density and flux in the ocean, and explore the challenges and limitations of these methods.




#### 4.1b Applications of Energy Conservation in Wave Dynamics

The principles of energy conservation are fundamental to the study of wave dynamics. They allow us to understand the behavior of waves in the ocean, and to predict the forces exerted by waves on ships and offshore energy systems. In this section, we will explore some of the key applications of energy conservation in wave dynamics.

#### 4.1b.1 Wave Energy Density and Flux

As we have seen in the previous sections, the concepts of wave energy density and flux are crucial in the study of wave dynamics. They allow us to quantify the energy content of a wave field, and to predict the rate at which energy is transported by the waves.

The energy density of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
E = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $\eta$ is the wave amplitude.

The energy flux of a wave is given by the equation:

$$
F = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2 \omega
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave.

These equations allow us to calculate the energy density and flux at any point in a wave field, and to predict the forces exerted by the waves on a ship or an offshore energy system.

#### 4.1b.2 Wave Interaction with Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

The principles of energy conservation are also crucial in understanding the interaction between waves and ships or offshore energy systems. When a wave interacts with a ship or an offshore energy system, energy is transferred from the wave to the system. This energy transfer can be calculated using the principles of energy conservation.

For example, consider a ship moving through a wave field. The ship experiences a force due to the wave, which can be calculated using the equation:

$$
F = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2 \omega
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, and $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave.

This force can be used to calculate the work done by the wave on the ship, which is equal to the change in the ship's kinetic energy. This allows us to predict the motion of the ship in the wave field.

Similarly, for offshore energy systems, the principles of energy conservation can be used to predict the forces exerted by the waves on the system, and to design the system for maximum efficiency and safety.

In conclusion, the principles of energy conservation are fundamental to the study of wave dynamics. They allow us to understand the behavior of waves in the ocean, and to predict the forces exerted by waves on ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 4.1c Energy Conservation in Wave Energy Systems

In the previous sections, we have discussed the principles of energy conservation and their applications in wave dynamics. In this section, we will delve deeper into the concept of energy conservation in wave energy systems.

#### 4.1c.1 Energy Conservation in Wave Energy Converters

Wave energy converters are devices that convert the kinetic energy of waves into electrical energy. These devices operate based on the principles of energy conservation. The energy input to the device is equal to the energy output, plus the energy lost due to friction and other losses.

The energy input to a wave energy converter can be calculated using the equation:

$$
E_{\text{in}} = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2 \omega
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, and $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave.

The energy output from the device is typically in the form of electrical energy, which can be calculated using the equation:

$$
E_{\text{out}} = \frac{1}{2}I^2R
$$

where $I$ is the current and $R$ is the resistance.

The energy lost due to friction and other losses can be calculated using the equation:

$$
E_{\text{loss}} = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2 \omega - \frac{1}{2}I^2R
$$

#### 4.1c.2 Energy Conservation in Wave Energy Storage

Wave energy can also be stored for later use. This is typically done using a wave energy storage device, such as a wave energy accumulator. The energy stored in a wave energy storage device can be calculated using the equation:

$$
E_{\text{stored}} = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2 \omega
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, and $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave.

The energy released from the storage device can be calculated using the same equation.

#### 4.1c.3 Energy Conservation in Wave Energy Transfer

Wave energy can also be transferred from one location to another. This is typically done using a wave energy transfer device, such as a wave energy transmitter. The energy transferred can be calculated using the equation:

$$
E_{\text{transferred}} = \frac{1}{2}\rho g \eta^2 \omega
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, and $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave.

The energy lost during the transfer can be calculated using the same equation.

In conclusion, the principles of energy conservation are fundamental to the understanding and design of wave energy systems. By applying these principles, we can design efficient and effective wave energy systems that can harness the power of the ocean waves.




#### 4.2a Definition of Wave Energy Density

Wave energy density is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves. It is defined as the amount of energy per unit area of the ocean surface. This energy is distributed throughout the water column and is a result of the kinetic and potential energy of the water particles.

The mean wave energy density per unit horizontal area, often denoted as `E`, is the sum of the mean kinetic energy density `E_kin` and the mean potential energy density `E_pot`. These quantities can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
E = E_{\text{kin}} + E_{\text{pot}}
$$

The mean kinetic energy density `E_kin` is given by:

$$
E_{\text{kin}} = \overline{\int_{-h}^0 \tfrac12 \rho \left[ \left| \mathbf{U} + \mathbf{u}_x \right|^2 + u_z^2 \right]\, \mathrm{d}z} - \int_{-h}^0 \tfrac12 \rho \left| \mathbf{U} \right|^2\, \mathrm{d}z
$$

where `σ` is the intrinsic frequency, `h` is the water depth, `ρ` is the density of the fluid, `U` is the velocity of the fluid, `u` is the velocity of the wave, `x` is the horizontal direction, `z` is the vertical direction, and `| |` denotes the magnitude of a vector.

The mean potential energy density `E_pot` is given by:

$$
E_{\text{pot}} = \overline{\int_{-h}^{\eta} \rho gz\,\mathrm{d}z} - \int_{-h}^0 \rho gz\, \mathrm{d}z
$$

where `η` is the wave amplitude, `g` is the acceleration due to gravity, and `z` is the vertical direction.

In the case of surface tension effects not being negligible, the potential and kinetic energy densities are modified to include a contribution from surface tension. This is given by:

$$
E_{\text{pot}} = \overline{\int_{-h}^{\eta} \rho gz + \gamma \kappa\,\mathrm{d}z} - \int_{-h}^0 \rho gz + \gamma \kappa\, \mathrm{d}z
$$

where `γ` is the surface tension coefficient and `κ` is the curvature of the wave surface.

The wave energy density is a crucial concept in the study of ocean waves. It provides a measure of the energy content of the waves, which is essential for understanding wave propagation, wave interaction with structures, and wave energy conversion.

#### 4.2b Calculation of Wave Energy Density

The calculation of wave energy density involves the integration of the kinetic and potential energy densities over the water column. This is typically done numerically, using methods such as the Gauss-Seidel method or the Runge-Kutta method.

The Gauss-Seidel method is an iterative technique for solving a system of linear equations. In the context of wave energy density, it can be used to solve the equations for the kinetic and potential energy densities. The method involves iteratively updating the values of the energy densities until they converge to a solution.

The Runge-Kutta method, on the other hand, is a numerical integration technique that can be used to approximate the integrals in the equations for the kinetic and potential energy densities. This method involves evaluating the integrands at several points within the integration interval, and then using these evaluations to approximate the integral.

The calculation of wave energy density can be complex, especially in the presence of surface tension effects. In such cases, it may be necessary to include additional terms in the equations for the kinetic and potential energy densities, as shown in the previous section.

The calculation of wave energy density is a crucial step in the study of ocean waves. It provides a quantitative measure of the energy content of the waves, which is essential for understanding wave propagation, wave interaction with structures, and wave energy conversion.

#### 4.2c Wave Energy Density in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave energy density is primarily due to the potential energy of the wave. This is because the kinetic energy density is proportional to the square of the wave amplitude, which is typically small in deep water. The potential energy density, on the other hand, is proportional to the wave amplitude and the wave frequency, both of which are significant in deep water.

The wave energy density in deep water can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
E = E_{\text{pot}} = \overline{\int_{-h}^{\eta} \rho gz + \gamma \kappa\,\mathrm{d}z} - \int_{-h}^0 \rho gz + \gamma \kappa\, \mathrm{d}z
$$

where `η` is the wave amplitude, `h` is the water depth, `g` is the acceleration due to gravity, `ρ` is the density of the fluid, `U` is the velocity of the fluid, `u` is the velocity of the wave, `x` is the horizontal direction, `z` is the vertical direction, `| |` denotes the magnitude of a vector, `σ` is the intrinsic frequency, `γ` is the surface tension coefficient, and `κ` is the curvature of the wave surface.

The wave energy density in deep water is a crucial concept in the study of ocean waves. It provides a measure of the energy content of the waves, which is essential for understanding wave propagation, wave interaction with structures, and wave energy conversion.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wave energy flux, which is another important aspect of wave energy in the ocean.

#### 4.3a Definition of Wave Energy Flux

Wave energy flux is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves. It is defined as the rate at which wave energy is transported across a unit area normal to the direction of propagation. In other words, it is the energy density of the wave multiplied by the wave's group velocity.

The wave energy flux, `S`, can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
S = E \cdot C_g
$$

where `E` is the wave energy density and `C_g` is the group velocity of the wave. The group velocity is the velocity at which the wave's energy is transported, and it is typically larger than the phase velocity of the wave.

The wave energy flux is a crucial concept in the study of ocean waves. It provides a measure of the energy transported by the wave, which is essential for understanding wave propagation, wave interaction with structures, and wave energy conversion.

In the next sections, we will discuss the calculation of wave energy flux and its implications for wave energy systems.

#### 4.3b Calculation of Wave Energy Flux

The calculation of wave energy flux involves the determination of the wave energy density and the group velocity of the wave. The wave energy density, `E`, has been discussed in the previous sections. The group velocity, `C_g`, can be calculated using the dispersion relation of the wave.

The dispersion relation is a mathematical relationship between the wave's frequency and its wave number, which is a measure of the wave's spatial frequency. The group velocity is the derivative of the dispersion relation with respect to the wave number.

For deep water, the dispersion relation is given by:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where `ω` is the frequency of the wave, `k` is the wave number, and `g` is the acceleration due to gravity. The group velocity, `C_g`, is then given by:

$$
C_g = \frac{1}{2}\sqrt{\frac{g}{k}}
$$

Substituting these expressions into the equation for wave energy flux, we obtain:

$$
S = E \cdot \frac{1}{2}\sqrt{\frac{g}{k}}
$$

This equation gives the wave energy flux in deep water. In the next section, we will discuss the calculation of wave energy flux in shallow water.

#### 4.3c Wave Energy Flux in Shallow Water

In shallow water, the wave energy flux is calculated differently due to the presence of the bottom boundary. The wave energy flux in shallow water, `S`, is given by:

$$
S = E \cdot C_g + \frac{1}{2} \rho g \eta \sqrt{g}
$$

where `E` is the wave energy density, `C_g` is the group velocity, `ρ` is the density of the fluid, `g` is the acceleration due to gravity, `η` is the wave amplitude, and `k` is the wave number. The first term on the right-hand side represents the energy flux due to the wave propagation, as in deep water. The second term represents the energy flux due to the wave reflection from the bottom boundary.

The wave energy flux in shallow water is a crucial concept in the study of ocean waves. It provides a measure of the energy transported by the wave, which is essential for understanding wave propagation, wave interaction with structures, and wave energy conversion.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of wave energy flux for wave energy systems.

#### 4.3d Wave Energy Flux and Wave Power

Wave power is a measure of the rate at which wave energy is absorbed or produced. It is typically expressed in watts per square meter (W/m²). The wave power, `P`, can be calculated from the wave energy flux, `S`, using the equation:

$$
P = S \cdot C_g
$$

where `C_g` is the group velocity of the wave. This equation shows that the wave power is proportional to the product of the wave energy flux and the group velocity.

The wave power is a crucial concept in the study of ocean waves. It provides a measure of the rate at which wave energy is absorbed or produced, which is essential for understanding wave propagation, wave interaction with structures, and wave energy conversion.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of wave power for wave energy systems.

We have explored the mathematical models that describe these phenomena, and how they are used to predict and understand wave behavior.

We have learned that wave energy density is a measure of the energy contained in a wave, and it is a crucial factor in determining the strength and impact of waves on ships and offshore structures. We have also seen how wave energy flux, or the rate of energy transfer, plays a key role in the propagation of waves and the distribution of energy in the ocean.

By understanding these concepts, we can better predict and manage the effects of ocean waves on ships and offshore energy systems. This knowledge is essential for the design and operation of these systems, and for the safety and efficiency of maritime operations.
