## Chapter: Wave Interactions with Structures

### Introduction

The study of wave interactions with structures is a critical aspect of ocean engineering. This chapter delves into the complex dynamics of wave-structure interactions, a phenomenon that is fundamental to the design and operation of various marine structures such as ships, offshore platforms, and coastal defenses.

Wave-structure interactions are governed by a multitude of factors, including the properties of the wave, the characteristics of the structure, and the environmental conditions. These interactions can lead to a variety of outcomes, ranging from the benign absorption of wave energy by a structure, to the catastrophic failure of a structure due to excessive wave loading.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe wave-structure interactions. These models, which are often expressed in terms of differential equations, provide a quantitative understanding of the wave-structure interaction process. We will also discuss the practical implications of these models, and how they can be used to predict and manage wave-structure interactions in real-world applications.

The study of wave-structure interactions is a challenging but rewarding field, offering the potential for significant advancements in ocean engineering. By understanding the principles and techniques presented in this chapter, readers will be well-equipped to tackle the complexities of wave-structure interactions in their own work.




#### 4.2b Measurement of Wave Energy Density

The measurement of wave energy density is a crucial aspect of oceanographic research. It allows us to quantify the energy content of ocean waves, which is essential for understanding wave propagation, wave-structure interaction, and wave energy conversion.

There are several methods for measuring wave energy density, each with its own advantages and limitations. One of the most common methods is the use of wave energy spectrum, which provides a frequency-dependent measure of the wave energy density. This method is particularly useful for studying the energy content of ocean waves at different frequencies.

Another method is the use of wave energy density meters, which are instruments that measure the wave energy density directly. These meters can be deployed on the ocean surface or on offshore structures, and they provide real-time measurements of the wave energy density.

In addition to these methods, there are also numerical models that can be used to estimate the wave energy density. These models use mathematical equations to simulate the propagation of ocean waves, and they can provide valuable insights into the wave energy density at different locations and at different times.

It's important to note that the measurement of wave energy density is a complex task that requires careful calibration and validation. The accuracy of these measurements can be affected by various factors, including the accuracy of the instruments, the quality of the data, and the assumptions made in the analysis.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these methods and discuss their principles, their applications, and their limitations. We will also discuss the challenges and the future directions in the field of wave energy density measurement.

#### 4.2c Wave Energy Density in Deep Ocean

The measurement of wave energy density in the deep ocean is a challenging task due to the harsh conditions and the lack of direct access. However, it is a crucial aspect of oceanographic research, as it allows us to understand the energy content of ocean waves at different depths and to study the effects of deep ocean waves on offshore structures and energy systems.

One of the main methods for measuring wave energy density in the deep ocean is the use of deep-ocean Assessment and Reporting of Tsunamis (DART) buoy systems. These systems consist of a bottom pressure recorder (BPR) anchored to the bottom of the sea floor, a moored surface buoy, and a transmission link that sends data from the BPR to the surface buoy. The surface buoy then sends the data by radio to satellites, which transmit it to the system office.

The DART buoy systems provide real-time measurements of the wave energy density at different depths. These measurements are crucial for understanding the propagation of deep ocean waves and for studying the effects of these waves on offshore structures and energy systems.

Another method for measuring wave energy density in the deep ocean is the use of wave energy spectrum. This method provides a frequency-dependent measure of the wave energy density, which can be used to study the energy content of ocean waves at different frequencies and at different depths.

In addition to these methods, there are also numerical models that can be used to estimate the wave energy density in the deep ocean. These models use mathematical equations to simulate the propagation of ocean waves, and they can provide valuable insights into the wave energy density at different depths and at different times.

It's important to note that the measurement of wave energy density in the deep ocean is a complex task that requires careful calibration and validation. The accuracy of these measurements can be affected by various factors, including the accuracy of the instruments, the quality of the data, and the assumptions made in the analysis.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these methods and discuss their principles, their applications, and their limitations. We will also discuss the challenges and the future directions in the field of wave energy density measurement in the deep ocean.

#### 4.3a Wave Energy Flux

Wave energy flux is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves. It is defined as the rate at which wave energy is transported across a unit area perpendicular to the direction of propagation. This concept is crucial for understanding the propagation of ocean waves and their interaction with offshore structures and energy systems.

The wave energy flux can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
F = \frac{1}{2} \rho g \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} \left| \eta \right|^2 c \, k \, \cos \left( k x - \omega t + \phi \right) \, \mathrm{d}x
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, $c$ is the phase speed of the wave, $k$ is the wave number, $\omega$ is the angular frequency, $t$ is time, and $\phi$ is the phase shift.

The wave energy flux is a vector quantity, and it is typically represented in the direction of the wave propagation. The direction of the wave energy flux can be determined by the Poynting vector, which is defined as:

$$
\mathbf{P} = \frac{1}{\rho g} \frac{\partial}{\partial x} \left( \frac{1}{2} \rho g \left| \eta \right|^2 c \, k \, \cos \left( k x - \omega t + \phi \right) \right)
$$

The Poynting vector provides a directional indication of the energy flow, and it is crucial for understanding the propagation of ocean waves.

The wave energy flux is a key parameter in the study of ocean waves. It allows us to quantify the energy content of ocean waves, to study the effects of waves on offshore structures and energy systems, and to understand the propagation of waves in the ocean.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the concept of wave energy flux and discuss its applications in the study of ocean waves. We will also discuss the methods for measuring wave energy flux and the challenges associated with these measurements.

#### 4.3b Measurement of Wave Energy Flux

The measurement of wave energy flux is a complex task due to the dynamic nature of ocean waves. However, it is a crucial aspect of oceanographic research, as it allows us to understand the energy content of ocean waves and their interaction with offshore structures and energy systems.

One of the main methods for measuring wave energy flux is the use of wave energy spectrum. This method provides a frequency-dependent measure of the wave energy flux, which can be used to study the energy content of ocean waves at different frequencies. The wave energy spectrum is typically represented as a function of the wave number $k$ or the angular frequency $\omega$.

Another method for measuring wave energy flux is the use of wave energy flux meters. These instruments are designed to measure the wave energy flux directly, and they can provide real-time measurements of the wave energy flux. Wave energy flux meters can be deployed on the ocean surface or on offshore structures, and they can provide valuable data for the study of ocean waves.

In addition to these methods, there are also numerical models that can be used to estimate the wave energy flux. These models use mathematical equations to simulate the propagation of ocean waves, and they can provide valuable insights into the wave energy flux at different locations and at different times.

It's important to note that the measurement of wave energy flux is a complex task that requires careful calibration and validation. The accuracy of these measurements can be affected by various factors, including the accuracy of the instruments, the quality of the data, and the assumptions made in the analysis.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these methods and discuss their principles, their applications, and their limitations. We will also discuss the challenges and the future directions in the field of wave energy flux measurement.

#### 4.3c Wave Energy Flux in Deep Ocean

The measurement of wave energy flux in the deep ocean is a challenging task due to the harsh conditions and the lack of direct access. However, it is a crucial aspect of oceanographic research, as it allows us to understand the energy content of deep ocean waves and their interaction with offshore structures and energy systems.

One of the main methods for measuring wave energy flux in the deep ocean is the use of deep-ocean Assessment and Reporting of Tsunamis (DART) buoy systems. These systems consist of a bottom pressure recorder (BPR) anchored to the bottom of the sea floor, a moored surface buoy, and a transmission link that sends data from the BPR to the surface buoy. The surface buoy then sends the data by radio to satellites, which transmit it to the system office.

The DART buoy systems provide real-time measurements of the wave energy flux at different depths in the ocean. These measurements are crucial for understanding the propagation of deep ocean waves and for studying the effects of these waves on offshore structures and energy systems.

Another method for measuring wave energy flux in the deep ocean is the use of wave energy spectrum. This method provides a frequency-dependent measure of the wave energy flux, which can be used to study the energy content of deep ocean waves at different frequencies. The wave energy spectrum is typically represented as a function of the wave number $k$ or the angular frequency $\omega$.

In addition to these methods, there are also numerical models that can be used to estimate the wave energy flux in the deep ocean. These models use mathematical equations to simulate the propagation of deep ocean waves, and they can provide valuable insights into the wave energy flux at different locations and at different times.

It's important to note that the measurement of wave energy flux in the deep ocean is a complex task that requires careful calibration and validation. The accuracy of these measurements can be affected by various factors, including the accuracy of the instruments, the quality of the data, and the assumptions made in the analysis.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these methods and discuss their principles, their applications, and their limitations. We will also discuss the challenges and the future directions in the field of wave energy flux measurement in the deep ocean.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of wave energy density and flux, a critical aspect of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern these phenomena, and how they interact with various factors such as wave height, wavelength, and frequency. 

We have also examined the mathematical models that describe wave energy density and flux, and how these models can be used to predict and analyze wave behavior. These models, while complex, provide a powerful tool for understanding and predicting the behavior of ocean waves, which is crucial for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

In addition, we have discussed the practical implications of wave energy density and flux, and how these concepts are applied in the real world. From the design of ships and offshore structures, to the prediction of wave behavior in extreme weather conditions, the understanding of wave energy density and flux is a vital tool in the arsenal of ocean engineers and scientists.

In conclusion, the study of wave energy density and flux is a complex but essential aspect of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. By understanding these phenomena, we can design more efficient ships and offshore structures, and predict and mitigate the effects of extreme weather conditions.

## Chapter 5: Wave Interaction with Ships

### Introduction

The interaction of waves with ships is a critical aspect of ocean engineering. This chapter, "Wave Interaction with Ships," delves into the complex dynamics of this interaction, exploring the fundamental principles and mathematical models that govern it. 

Ships, as floating structures, are subject to the forces exerted by waves. These forces can be beneficial, propelling the ship forward, or detrimental, causing stress and strain that can lead to structural failure. Understanding the dynamics of wave interaction with ships is therefore crucial for the design and operation of ships, as well as for the safety of those on board.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe wave interaction with ships. These models, expressed in terms of wave height, wavelength, and ship dimensions, provide a quantitative understanding of the forces exerted by waves on ships. We will also discuss the practical implications of these models, demonstrating how they can be used to predict wave forces on ships and to design ships that can withstand these forces.

We will also delve into the effects of wave interaction on ship motion, including heave, pitch, and roll. These effects can be significant, particularly in extreme weather conditions, and understanding them is crucial for the design of ships that can maintain stability and control in these conditions.

Finally, we will discuss the role of wave interaction in ship design and operation, highlighting the importance of considering wave interaction in the design of ships and in the planning of ship operations.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of wave interaction with ships, combining theoretical discussion with practical examples and case studies. It is designed to be accessible to both students and professionals in the field of ocean engineering, and to provide the knowledge and tools needed to understand and predict wave interaction with ships.




#### 4.2c Wave Energy Density in Deep Ocean

The deep ocean, defined as the ocean floor deeper than 200 meters, is a vast and complex environment where waves play a crucial role. The wave energy density in the deep ocean is a key parameter in understanding the dynamics of the ocean and its impact on marine structures and ecosystems.

The measurement of wave energy density in the deep ocean is a challenging task due to the harsh conditions and the lack of direct access. However, several methods and technologies have been developed to estimate the wave energy density in this environment.

One of the most common methods is the use of wave energy spectrum, which provides a frequency-dependent measure of the wave energy density. This method is particularly useful for studying the energy content of ocean waves at different frequencies. However, the application of this method in the deep ocean is limited due to the difficulty in obtaining accurate spectral measurements in this environment.

Another method is the use of wave energy density meters, which are instruments that measure the wave energy density directly. These meters can be deployed on the ocean floor or on underwater structures, and they provide real-time measurements of the wave energy density. However, the deployment of these instruments in the deep ocean is challenging and expensive.

In addition to these methods, there are also numerical models that can be used to estimate the wave energy density in the deep ocean. These models use mathematical equations to simulate the propagation of ocean waves, and they can provide valuable insights into the wave energy density at different locations and at different times. However, the accuracy of these models is highly dependent on the quality of the input data and the accuracy of the mathematical representations of the physical processes involved.

The wave energy density in the deep ocean is a key parameter in understanding the dynamics of the ocean and its impact on marine structures and ecosystems. However, further research is needed to improve our understanding and ability to measure and model the wave energy density in this environment.




#### 4.3a Definition of Wave Energy Flux

Wave energy flux is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves. It is defined as the rate at which wave energy is transported across a unit area normal to the direction of propagation. This concept is crucial in understanding the dynamics of ocean waves and their impact on marine structures and ecosystems.

The wave energy flux, denoted as $E_f$, is given by the product of the wave energy density $E_w$ and the wave propagation speed $c$:

$$
E_f = E_w \cdot c
$$

The wave energy density $E_w$ is a measure of the energy content of the wave at a given point in the ocean. It is typically measured in units of joules per meter cubed (J/m^3). The wave propagation speed $c$ is the speed at which the wave energy is transported through the ocean. It is typically measured in units of meters per second (m/s).

The wave energy flux is a vector quantity, with the direction of the vector indicating the direction of energy transport and the magnitude of the vector indicating the rate of energy transport. The direction of the wave energy flux is typically taken to be the direction of the wave propagation, which is the direction of the wave's motion.

The wave energy flux is a key parameter in understanding the dynamics of the ocean and its impact on marine structures and ecosystems. It is used in a variety of applications, including the design of offshore structures, the prediction of wave-induced loads on these structures, and the study of wave-wave interactions in the deep ocean.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the concept of wave energy flux, exploring its properties, its measurement, and its applications in the study of ocean waves.

#### 4.3b Calculation of Wave Energy Flux

The calculation of wave energy flux involves the determination of the wave energy density and the wave propagation speed. The wave energy density $E_w$ can be calculated using the wave energy spectrum, which provides a frequency-dependent measure of the wave energy density. The wave propagation speed $c$ can be determined using the dispersion relation, which relates the wave frequency to the wave number and the water depth.

The wave energy spectrum $S(f)$ is defined as the spectral density of the wave energy, where $f$ is the frequency of the wave. The wave energy spectrum is typically estimated from the Fourier transform of the wave time series. The wave energy density $E_w$ can then be calculated as:

$$
E_w = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} S(f) df
$$

The dispersion relation for deep water is given by:

$$
\omega = \sqrt{gk}
$$

where $\omega$ is the angular frequency of the wave, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $k$ is the wave number. The wave propagation speed $c$ can then be calculated as:

$$
c = \frac{\omega}{k} = \sqrt{\frac{g}{k}}
$$

Substituting the expressions for $E_w$ and $c$ into the definition of wave energy flux, we obtain:

$$
E_f = \int_{-\infty}^{\infty} S(f) \sqrt{gk} df
$$

This equation provides a method for calculating the wave energy flux. However, it should be noted that the wave energy spectrum and the dispersion relation are idealized models, and the actual wave energy flux in the ocean may deviate from these models due to various factors, including the presence of waves of different frequencies, the effects of wave-wave interactions, and the influence of the ocean bottom and the atmosphere. Therefore, the calculation of wave energy flux in the ocean is a complex task that requires careful consideration of these factors.

In the next section, we will discuss some of the methods and technologies that are used to measure wave energy flux in the ocean.

#### 4.3c Wave Energy Flux in Deep Ocean

The calculation of wave energy flux in the deep ocean is a complex task due to the unique characteristics of the deep ocean environment. The deep ocean is characterized by large water depths, low temperatures, high pressure, and sparse population. These conditions significantly influence the wave energy flux in the deep ocean.

The wave energy flux in the deep ocean can be calculated using the same approach as in shallow water, with some modifications to account for the effects of deep water. The wave energy spectrum $S(f)$ and the dispersion relation $\omega = \sqrt{gk}$ are still applicable, but the wave propagation speed $c$ is now given by:

$$
c = \frac{\omega}{k} = \sqrt{\frac{g}{k}}
$$

However, the wave energy spectrum and the dispersion relation are idealized models, and the actual wave energy flux in the deep ocean may deviate from these models due to various factors, including the effects of deep water.

One of the key factors that influence the wave energy flux in the deep ocean is the water depth. As the water depth increases, the wave energy flux decreases due to the increased path length that the wave energy must travel. This effect is particularly pronounced for deep ocean waves, where the wave energy flux can be significantly lower than in shallow water.

Another important factor is the wave frequency. In the deep ocean, the wave frequency is typically lower than in shallow water due to the increased water depth. This results in a lower wave energy flux, as the wave energy density is proportional to the square of the wave frequency.

The wave energy flux in the deep ocean is also influenced by the wave direction. In the deep ocean, the wave direction can be significantly different from the wave direction in shallow water due to the effects of deep water. This can result in a change in the wave energy flux, as the wave energy flux is proportional to the wave direction.

In addition to these factors, the wave energy flux in the deep ocean is also influenced by the wave-wave interactions, the effects of the ocean bottom and the atmosphere, and the presence of waves of different frequencies. Therefore, the calculation of wave energy flux in the deep ocean is a complex task that requires careful consideration of these factors.

In the next section, we will discuss some of the methods and technologies that are used to measure wave energy flux in the deep ocean.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the complex world of wave energy density and flux, two fundamental concepts in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. We have explored the mathematical models that describe these phenomena, and how they are influenced by various factors such as wave height, frequency, and water depth. 

We have also discussed the importance of these concepts in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. Understanding wave energy density and flux can help engineers design more efficient and robust systems, and can also aid in predicting and mitigating the effects of extreme weather events.

In conclusion, wave energy density and flux are crucial concepts in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. They provide a quantitative measure of the energy contained in a wave, and the rate at which this energy is transported. By understanding these concepts, we can better design and operate ships and offshore energy systems in the face of the ever-changing ocean conditions.

## Chapter: Wave Energy Conversion

### Introduction

The ocean is a vast source of energy, and harnessing this energy has been a topic of interest for many years. One of the most promising ways to harness this energy is through wave energy conversion. This chapter, "Wave Energy Conversion," will delve into the intricacies of this process, exploring the principles behind it, the technologies used, and the potential applications of wave energy conversion in the field of ocean engineering.

Wave energy conversion is the process of converting the kinetic energy of ocean waves into other forms of energy, such as electricity. This energy can then be used to power various devices and systems, from small-scale applications like buoys and lighthouses, to large-scale applications like offshore wind farms. The potential for wave energy conversion is vast, and it is a field that is constantly evolving as engineers and scientists work to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of wave energy conversion technologies.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles behind wave energy conversion, including the physics of wave propagation and the mathematical models used to describe wave energy. We will also delve into the various technologies used in wave energy conversion, from simple mechanical devices to complex electrical systems. We will discuss the advantages and disadvantages of these technologies, and how they are used in different applications.

Finally, we will explore the potential applications of wave energy conversion in the field of ocean engineering. This includes the use of wave energy conversion in offshore structures, such as oil rigs and wind farms, as well as the potential for wave energy conversion to power autonomous underwater vehicles (AUVs) and other marine equipment.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of wave energy conversion, its principles, technologies, and applications. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of ocean engineering, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge and tools you need to understand and work with wave energy conversion.




#### 4.3b Measurement of Wave Energy Flux

The measurement of wave energy flux is a complex task that requires sophisticated instrumentation and data processing techniques. The most common method for measuring wave energy flux is through the use of wave radar, a technology that utilizes radar waves to measure the height and direction of waves on the ocean surface.

Wave radar operates in the vertical mode at GHz frequencies, making it less affected by fog and water spray compared to laser rangefinders. The radar waveform used is typically a continuous wave frequency modulated (CWFM) or pulsed radar waveform, which provides range resolution. The beam diverges as it propagates, resulting in a larger footprint with increasing range. However, the area of the footprint increases proportionally to the square of the range.

One example of a wave radar used for wave energy flux measurement is the Miros SM-094, which is designed to measure waves and water level, including tides. This sensor is used as an air gap (bridge clearance) sensor in NOAA's PORTS system. Another example is the WaveRadar REX, a derivative of a Rosemount tank radar.

The measurement of wave energy flux involves collecting data on the elevation of the surface of the water at three or more locations. This data is then used to compute a directional spectrum of wave height, which is similar to the algorithm used to generate a directional spectrum from data on heave (vertical motion), pitch, and roll at a single location, as provided by a disc-shaped wave buoy. An array of three vertical radars, having footprints at the vertices of a horizontal, equilateral triangle, can provide the necessary data on water surface elevation.

The directional wave energy flux can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
E_f = \int_{-\pi}^{\pi} E_w(\theta) \cdot c(\theta) \cdot P(\theta) \cdot d\theta
$$

where $E_w(\theta)$ is the wave energy density as a function of direction, $c(\theta)$ is the wave propagation speed as a function of direction, $P(\theta)$ is the wave power as a function of direction, and $d\theta$ is the differential in direction.

In the next section, we will discuss the applications of wave energy flux measurement in the study of ocean waves and their impact on marine structures and ecosystems.

#### 4.3c Wave Energy Flux in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave energy flux is primarily influenced by the wave energy density and the wave propagation speed. The wave energy density is a function of the wave height and the wave propagation speed is determined by the water depth and the wave frequency. 

The wave energy density in deep water can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
E_w = \frac{1}{2} \rho g H^2
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the water, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $H$ is the wave height.

The wave propagation speed in deep water is given by the equation:

$$
c = \sqrt{\frac{g \cdot \lambda}{2 \cdot \pi}}
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavelength of the wave.

Substituting these equations into the wave energy flux equation, we get:

$$
E_f = \int_{-\pi}^{\pi} \frac{1}{2} \rho g H^2 \cdot \sqrt{\frac{g \cdot \lambda}{2 \cdot \pi}} \cdot P(\theta) \cdot d\theta
$$

This equation shows that the wave energy flux in deep water is primarily determined by the wave height, the wavelength, and the power distribution as a function of direction. 

In deep water, the wave energy flux is typically higher than in shallow water due to the longer wavelengths and higher wave propagation speeds. However, the wave energy flux can also be influenced by other factors such as the presence of currents and the topography of the ocean floor.

The measurement of wave energy flux in deep water is a challenging task due to the large area covered by the wave radar footprint. However, advancements in technology and data processing techniques have made it possible to accurately measure the wave energy flux in deep water.

In the next section, we will discuss the impact of wave energy flux on offshore energy systems and marine structures.




#### 4.3c Effects of Wave Energy Flux on Wave Dynamics

The wave energy flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It is the rate at which wave energy is transported across a given area, and it is a key factor in determining the strength and direction of wave propagation. In this section, we will explore the effects of wave energy flux on wave dynamics, focusing on the role of wave energy flux in wave propagation and the impact of wave energy flux on wave breaking.

#### 4.3c.1 Wave Propagation

Wave propagation is the movement of waves through a medium, in this case, the ocean. The wave energy flux is a key factor in determining the direction and strength of wave propagation. As mentioned in the previous section, the wave energy flux can be calculated using the equation:

$$
E_f = \int_{-\pi}^{\pi} E_w(\theta) \cdot c(\theta) \cdot P(\theta) \cdot d\theta
$$

where $E_w(\theta)$ is the wave energy density as a function of direction, $c(\theta)$ is the wave propagation speed, and $P(\theta)$ is the wave propagation direction. The wave energy flux is directly proportional to the product of the wave energy density and the wave propagation speed. This means that an increase in wave energy density or wave propagation speed will result in an increase in wave energy flux.

The wave energy flux also plays a crucial role in determining the direction of wave propagation. As the wave energy flux is proportional to the product of the wave energy density and the wave propagation direction, an increase in the wave energy density in a particular direction will result in an increase in the wave energy flux in that direction. This can lead to a strengthening of the wave propagation in that direction, potentially leading to the formation of wave fronts and the propagation of waves across large distances.

#### 4.3c.2 Wave Breaking

Wave breaking is a phenomenon that occurs when the wave energy density exceeds a certain threshold. This threshold is typically associated with the wave steepness, which is the ratio of the wave height to the wave length. When the wave steepness exceeds a certain value, the wave can break, leading to the transfer of wave energy from the wave to the surrounding water.

The wave energy flux plays a crucial role in wave breaking. As the wave energy flux is directly proportional to the wave energy density, an increase in the wave energy density can lead to an increase in the likelihood of wave breaking. This is because a higher wave energy density means that more wave energy is available to be transferred to the surrounding water when the wave breaks.

In conclusion, the wave energy flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of ocean waves. It is a key factor in determining the direction and strength of wave propagation, and it also plays a crucial role in wave breaking. Understanding the effects of wave energy flux on wave dynamics is crucial for predicting and managing the impact of waves on ships and offshore energy systems.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fundamental concepts of wave energy density and flux, and their importance in understanding the interaction between ocean waves and ships or offshore energy systems. We have learned that wave energy density is a measure of the energy contained in a wave, while wave energy flux is a measure of the energy transfer rate of a wave. These concepts are crucial in the design and analysis of ships and offshore energy systems, as they allow us to quantify the effects of ocean waves on these structures.

We have also discussed the various factors that influence wave energy density and flux, such as wave height, wavelength, and frequency. These factors are interconnected and can significantly impact the energy content and transfer rate of a wave. By understanding these factors, we can better predict and mitigate the effects of ocean waves on ships and offshore energy systems.

Furthermore, we have explored the different methods for measuring wave energy density and flux, including the use of wave energy spectrum and wave energy flux density. These methods provide a more detailed understanding of the wave energy and its distribution, allowing us to make more accurate predictions and design more efficient structures.

In conclusion, wave energy density and flux are essential concepts in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. By understanding these concepts and their influencing factors, we can better design and analyze these structures to withstand the effects of ocean waves.

We will begin by discussing the basics of wave energy dissipation, including the different types of energy dissipation mechanisms and their effects on waves. We will then delve into the specific applications of wave energy dissipation in the context of ships and offshore energy systems. ## Chapter 5: Wave Energy Dissipation:




### Introduction

Wave momentum flux is a fundamental concept in the study of wave dynamics, and it plays a significant role in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. It is a measure of the transfer of momentum between the water and the atmosphere, and it is a key factor in determining the strength and direction of ocean currents.

The study of wave momentum flux is essential for several reasons. Firstly, it provides insights into the dynamics of ocean waves, which is crucial for understanding the behavior of ships and offshore energy systems. Secondly, it helps in predicting the impact of waves on these systems, which is vital for their design and operation. Lastly, it contributes to our understanding of the larger ocean-atmosphere system, which is essential for climate studies and weather forecasting.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of wave momentum flux, including its definition, calculation, and significance. We will also discuss the various factors that influence wave momentum flux, such as wave height, wave direction, and water depth. Furthermore, we will examine the role of wave momentum flux in the generation of ocean currents and its implications for ship navigation and offshore energy systems.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a comprehensive understanding of wave momentum flux and its importance in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the specific applications of wave momentum flux in these areas.




### Section: 5.1 Momentum Conservation for Waves:

#### 5.1a Momentum Conservation Equations for Waves

The principle of momentum conservation is a fundamental concept in physics that states that the total momentum of a closed system remains constant unless acted upon by an external force. This principle is also applicable to waves, and it forms the basis of the Cauchy momentum equation.

The Cauchy momentum equation can be expressed as:

$$
\vec p(t+\Delta t) - \vec p(t) = \Delta t \vec\bar F
$$

where $\vec p(t)$ is the momentum in time `t`, and $\vec\bar F$ is the force averaged over $\Delta t$. Dividing by $\Delta t$ and passing to the limit $\Delta t \to 0$, we get the derivative:

$$
\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \vec F
$$

Let us analyze each side of the equation.

#### Right side

The right side of the equation represents the total force acting on the system. This force can be split into body forces $\vec F_m$ and surface forces $\vec F_p$. The surface forces act on the walls of the cubic fluid element. For each wall, the "X" component of these forces was marked in the figure with a cubic element (in the form of a product of stress and surface area e.g. $-\sigma_{xx} \, dy \, dz$ with units $\mathrm{Pa\cdot m\cdot m = \frac{N}{m^2} \cdot m^2 = N}$).

Adding forces (their "X" components) acting on each of the cube walls, we get:

$$
F_p^x =
\left(\sigma_{xx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{xx}}{\partial x}dx\right)dy\,dz
-\sigma_{xx}dy\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{yx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{yx}}{\partial y}dy\right)dx\,dz
-\sigma_{yx}dx\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{zx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{zx}}{\partial z}dz\right)dx\,dy
-\sigma_{zx}dx\,dy
$$

After ordering $F_p^x$ and performing similar reasoning for components $F_p^y, F_p^z$, we get:

$$
\vec F_p = \nabla \cdot \vec\sigma
$$

where $\vec\sigma$ is the stress tensor.

#### Left side

The left side of the equation represents the change in momentum over time. This can be expressed as:

$$
\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v)
$$

where $\vec v$ is the velocity vector.

By equating the right and left sides of the equation, we obtain the momentum conservation equation for waves:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v) = \nabla \cdot \vec\sigma
$$

This equation is a powerful tool for studying wave momentum flux, as it allows us to analyze the forces acting on a wave system and how they affect the system's momentum. In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the implications of this equation and its applications in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.1b Wave Momentum Flux and Energy Transfer

The concept of wave momentum flux is closely related to the concept of energy transfer. As we have seen in the previous section, the momentum conservation equation for waves can be expressed as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v) = \nabla \cdot \vec\sigma
$$

This equation can also be interpreted in terms of energy transfer. The left side of the equation represents the rate of change of momentum, which is equivalent to the rate of change of energy. The right side of the equation represents the divergence of the stress tensor, which is equivalent to the force per unit area.

The stress tensor $\vec\sigma$ can be decomposed into two parts: the first part represents the normal stress, which is the dot product of the stress tensor and the normal vector; and the second part represents the shear stress, which is the cross product of the stress tensor and the normal vector. The normal stress is equivalent to the force per unit area in the direction of the normal vector, while the shear stress is equivalent to the force per unit area in the direction perpendicular to the normal vector.

Therefore, the momentum conservation equation for waves can be rewritten as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v) = \nabla \cdot (\vec\sigma_n + \vec\sigma_s)
$$

where $\vec\sigma_n$ is the normal stress tensor and $\vec\sigma_s$ is the shear stress tensor.

This equation shows that the rate of change of momentum is equal to the divergence of the normal and shear stress tensors. This means that the rate of change of momentum is equal to the rate of energy transfer. In other words, the momentum flux is equivalent to the energy flux.

This relationship between wave momentum flux and energy transfer is crucial in understanding the behavior of waves in the ocean. It allows us to analyze the forces acting on a wave system and how they affect the system's energy. This is particularly important in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, where the transfer of energy between waves and these systems plays a crucial role.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of wave momentum flux and its applications in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.1c Wave Momentum Flux and Wave Propagation

The concept of wave momentum flux is not only crucial in understanding the energy transfer between waves and other systems, but it also plays a significant role in the propagation of waves. The momentum conservation equation for waves, as we have seen, can be rewritten as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v) = \nabla \cdot (\vec\sigma_n + \vec\sigma_s)
$$

This equation can be used to analyze the propagation of waves in the ocean. The left side of the equation represents the rate of change of momentum, which is equivalent to the rate of change of energy. The right side of the equation represents the divergence of the stress tensor, which is equivalent to the force per unit area.

The stress tensor $\vec\sigma$ can be decomposed into two parts: the normal stress, which is the dot product of the stress tensor and the normal vector, and the shear stress, which is the cross product of the stress tensor and the normal vector. The normal stress is equivalent to the force per unit area in the direction of the normal vector, while the shear stress is equivalent to the force per unit area in the direction perpendicular to the normal vector.

Therefore, the momentum conservation equation for waves can be rewritten as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v) = \nabla \cdot (\vec\sigma_n + \vec\sigma_s)
$$

where $\vec\sigma_n$ is the normal stress tensor and $\vec\sigma_s$ is the shear stress tensor.

This equation shows that the rate of change of momentum is equal to the divergence of the normal and shear stress tensors. This means that the rate of change of momentum is equal to the rate of energy transfer. In other words, the momentum flux is equivalent to the energy flux.

This relationship between wave momentum flux and energy transfer is crucial in understanding the propagation of waves in the ocean. It allows us to analyze the forces acting on a wave system and how they affect the system's energy. This is particularly important in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, where the transfer of energy between waves and these systems plays a crucial role.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of wave momentum flux and its applications in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

### Section: 5.1 Momentum Conservation for Waves:

#### 5.1a Momentum Conservation Equations for Waves

The principle of momentum conservation is a fundamental concept in physics that states that the total momentum of a closed system remains constant unless acted upon by an external force. This principle is also applicable to waves, and it forms the basis of the Cauchy momentum equation.

The Cauchy momentum equation can be expressed as:

$$
\vec p(t+\Delta t) - \vec p(t) = \Delta t \vec\bar F
$$

where $\vec p(t)$ is the momentum in time `t`, and $\vec\bar F$ is the force averaged over $\Delta t$. Dividing by $\Delta t$ and passing to the limit $\Delta t \to 0$, we get the derivative:

$$
\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \vec F
$$

Let us analyze each side of the equation.

#### Right side

The right side of the equation represents the total force acting on the system. This force can be split into body forces $\vec F_m$ and surface forces $\vec F_p$. The surface forces act on the walls of the cubic fluid element. For each wall, the "X" component of these forces was marked in the figure with a cubic element (in the form of a product of stress and surface area e.g. $-\sigma_{xx} \, dy \, dz$ with units $\mathrm{Pa\cdot m\cdot m = \frac{N}{m^2} \cdot m^2 = N}$).

Adding forces (their "X" components) acting on each of the cube walls, we get:

$$
F_p^x =
\left(\sigma_{xx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{xx}}{\partial x}dx\right)dy\,dz
-\sigma_{xx}dy\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{yx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{yx}}{\partial y}dy\right)dx\,dz
-\sigma_{yx}dx\,dz
+\left(\sigma_{zx}+\frac{\partial\sigma_{zx}}{\partial z}dz\right)dx\,dy
-\sigma_{zx}dx\,dy
$$

After ordering $F_p^x$ and performing similar reasoning for components $F_p^y, F_p^z$, we get:

$$
\vec F_p = \nabla \cdot \vec\sigma
$$

where $\vec\sigma$ is the stress tensor.

#### Left side

The left side of the equation represents the change in momentum over time. This can be expressed as:

$$
\frac{d\vec p}{dt} = \frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v)
$$

where $\vec v$ is the velocity vector. The first term represents the change in momentum due to a change in time, while the second term represents the change in momentum due to a change in velocity.

Substituting the expressions for the right and left sides of the equation, we get:

$$
\nabla \cdot \vec\sigma = \frac{\partial \vec p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\vec p \vec v)
$$

This equation is known as the Cauchy momentum equation, and it represents the conservation of momentum for waves. It states that the change in momentum of a system is equal to the sum of the forces acting on the system. This equation is fundamental in the study of wave dynamics and is used to derive many important wave equations, including the wave momentum flux equation.

#### 5.1b Applications of Momentum Conservation in Wave Dynamics

The principle of momentum conservation is a powerful tool in the study of wave dynamics. It allows us to derive important equations that describe the behavior of waves, such as the wave momentum flux equation. This equation is particularly useful in understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

The wave momentum flux equation can be derived from the Cauchy momentum equation by considering the forces acting on a small volume of fluid. This equation describes the flux of momentum in a wave, and it is given by:

$$
\vec F = \rho \frac{\partial \vec v}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (\rho \vec v \vec v)
$$

where $\rho$ is the density of the fluid, $\vec v$ is the velocity vector, and $\vec F$ is the force vector. This equation is used to describe the momentum transfer between waves and ships or offshore energy systems.

In the context of ship dynamics, the wave momentum flux equation can be used to understand the forces acting on a ship due to waves. This is particularly important in the design of ships, as it allows engineers to predict the behavior of a ship in waves and design ships that can withstand these forces.

In the context of offshore energy systems, the wave momentum flux equation is used to understand the forces acting on these systems due to waves. This is crucial in the design and operation of these systems, as it allows engineers to predict the behavior of these systems in waves and design systems that can withstand these forces.

In conclusion, the principle of momentum conservation is a fundamental concept in the study of wave dynamics. It allows us to derive important equations, such as the wave momentum flux equation, which are crucial in understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.




### Section: 5.1 Momentum Conservation for Waves:

#### 5.1a Wave Momentum Conservation Equation

The conservation of momentum is a fundamental principle in physics that states that the total momentum of a closed system remains constant unless acted upon by an external force. This principle is also applicable to waves, and it is particularly important in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

The momentum conservation equation for waves can be derived from the Navier-Stokes equations, which describe the motion of fluid substances. In the case of waves, the fluid is the water in the ocean, and the motion is the propagation of the waves. The Navier-Stokes equations can be simplified under certain conditions, such as the assumption of incompressibility and the neglect of viscosity, to yield the wave momentum conservation equation.

The wave momentum conservation equation can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec{p}}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot \vec{F} = 0
$$

where $\vec{p}$ is the wave momentum and $\vec{F}$ is the wave force. The wave momentum is defined as the product of the wave amplitude and the wave vector, $\vec{p} = a \vec{k}$. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency, $\vec{F} = a \omega \vec{k}$.

The wave momentum conservation equation states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. This equation is a statement of the conservation of momentum for waves, and it is a key tool in the study of wave-structure interaction.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of the wave momentum conservation equation for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.1b Wave Momentum Conservation in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave momentum conservation equation takes on a simpler form due to the absence of bottom friction and the assumption of linear wave theory. The wave momentum conservation equation in deep water can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec{p}}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot \vec{F} = 0
$$

where $\vec{p}$ is the wave momentum and $\vec{F}$ is the wave force. The wave momentum is defined as the product of the wave amplitude and the wave vector, $\vec{p} = a \vec{k}$. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency, $\vec{F} = a \omega \vec{k}$.

The wave momentum conservation equation in deep water states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. This equation is a statement of the conservation of momentum for waves in deep water, and it is a key tool in the study of wave-structure interaction in deep water.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of the wave momentum conservation equation in deep water for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.1c Wave Momentum Conservation in Shallow Water

In shallow water, the wave momentum conservation equation becomes more complex due to the presence of bottom friction and the non-linear nature of wave propagation. The wave momentum conservation equation in shallow water can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec{p}}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot \vec{F} = \vec{F}_{bf}
$$

where $\vec{p}$ is the wave momentum, $\vec{F}$ is the wave force, and $\vec{F}_{bf}$ is the bottom friction force. The wave momentum is defined as the product of the wave amplitude and the wave vector, $\vec{p} = a \vec{k}$. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency, $\vec{F} = a \omega \vec{k}$. The bottom friction force is a function of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the bottom friction coefficient, $\vec{F}_{bf} = c_f a \vec{k}$.

The wave momentum conservation equation in shallow water states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force plus the bottom friction force. This equation is a statement of the conservation of momentum for waves in shallow water, and it is a key tool in the study of wave-structure interaction in shallow water.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of the wave momentum conservation equation in shallow water for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.1d Wave Momentum Conservation in Wave Energy Systems

In wave energy systems, the wave momentum conservation equation plays a crucial role in understanding the interaction between waves and energy converters. The wave momentum conservation equation in wave energy systems can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial \vec{p}}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot \vec{F} = \vec{F}_{ec}
$$

where $\vec{p}$ is the wave momentum, $\vec{F}$ is the wave force, and $\vec{F}_{ec}$ is the energy converter force. The wave momentum is defined as the product of the wave amplitude and the wave vector, $\vec{p} = a \vec{k}$. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency, $\vec{F} = a \omega \vec{k}$. The energy converter force is a function of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the energy converter efficiency, $\vec{F}_{ec} = \eta a \vec{k}$.

The wave momentum conservation equation in wave energy systems states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force plus the energy converter force. This equation is a statement of the conservation of momentum for waves in wave energy systems, and it is a key tool in the study of wave-structure interaction in wave energy systems.

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of the wave momentum conservation equation in wave energy systems for the design and operation of wave energy converters.




#### 5.2a Definition of Wave Momentum Density

Wave momentum density is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is defined as the amount of momentum per unit area of the wave. In other words, it is the wave momentum divided by the wave area. Mathematically, it can be expressed as:

$$
m = \frac{p}{A}
$$

where $m$ is the wave momentum density, $p$ is the wave momentum, and $A$ is the wave area.

The wave momentum density is a vector quantity, with both magnitude and direction. The direction of the wave momentum density vector is the same as the direction of the wave propagation. The magnitude of the wave momentum density vector is proportional to the wave amplitude and the wave frequency.

The wave momentum density is a crucial concept in the study of wave-structure interaction. It is the force per unit area exerted by the wave on the structure. This force can cause stress and strain in the structure, which can lead to fatigue and failure. Therefore, understanding the wave momentum density is essential for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

In the next section, we will discuss the calculation of the wave momentum density and its implications for wave-structure interaction.

#### 5.2b Calculation of Wave Momentum Density

The calculation of wave momentum density involves the integration of the wave momentum over the wave area. This can be done using the wave momentum conservation equation, which states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. 

The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency. The wave vector is the wave propagation direction multiplied by the wave number. The wave number is the inverse of the wavelength. The wave frequency is the inverse of the wave period. 

The wave momentum conservation equation can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot F = 0
$$

where $p$ is the wave momentum, $F$ is the wave force, and $\nabla \cdot$ denotes the divergence operator. 

The wave momentum density $m$ is then given by the integration of this equation over the wave area $A$:

$$
m = \int_A \frac{\partial p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot F \, dA = \int_A \frac{\partial (p/A)}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (F/A) \, dA
$$

This equation shows that the wave momentum density is the rate of change of the wave momentum density plus the divergence of the wave force density. The wave force density is the wave force divided by the wave area. 

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of this equation for wave-structure interaction.

#### 5.2c Wave Momentum Density in Deep Water

In deep water, the wave momentum density is particularly important due to the absence of bottom friction and the assumption of linear wave theory. The wave momentum density in deep water can be calculated using the wave momentum conservation equation, which states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. 

The wave force in deep water is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency. The wave vector is the wave propagation direction multiplied by the wave number. The wave number is the inverse of the wavelength. The wave frequency is the inverse of the wave period. 

The wave momentum conservation equation in deep water can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot F = 0
$$

where $p$ is the wave momentum, $F$ is the wave force, and $\nabla \cdot$ denotes the divergence operator. 

The wave momentum density $m$ in deep water is then given by the integration of this equation over the wave area $A$:

$$
m = \int_A \frac{\partial p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot F \, dA = \int_A \frac{\partial (p/A)}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (F/A) \, dA
$$

This equation shows that the wave momentum density in deep water is the rate of change of the wave momentum density plus the divergence of the wave force density. The wave force density in deep water is the wave force divided by the wave area. 

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of this equation for wave-structure interaction in deep water.

#### 5.2d Wave Momentum Density in Shallow Water

In shallow water, the wave momentum density is influenced by the bottom friction and the assumption of nonlinear wave theory. The wave momentum density in shallow water can be calculated using the wave momentum conservation equation, which states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. 

The wave force in shallow water is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency. The wave vector is the wave propagation direction multiplied by the wave number. The wave number is the inverse of the wavelength. The wave frequency is the inverse of the wave period. 

The wave momentum conservation equation in shallow water can be written as:

$$
\frac{\partial p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot F = 0
$$

where $p$ is the wave momentum, $F$ is the wave force, and $\nabla \cdot$ denotes the divergence operator. 

The wave momentum density $m$ in shallow water is then given by the integration of this equation over the wave area $A$:

$$
m = \int_A \frac{\partial p}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot F \, dA = \int_A \frac{\partial (p/A)}{\partial t} + \nabla \cdot (F/A) \, dA
$$

This equation shows that the wave momentum density in shallow water is the rate of change of the wave momentum density plus the divergence of the wave force density. The wave force density in shallow water is the wave force divided by the wave area. 

In the next section, we will discuss the implications of this equation for wave-structure interaction in shallow water.

## Chapter: Wave-Structure Interaction

### Introduction

The interaction between waves and structures is a critical aspect of ocean engineering. This chapter, "Wave-Structure Interaction," delves into the complex dynamics of this interaction, providing a comprehensive understanding of the principles and mechanisms involved. 

Wave-structure interaction is a fundamental concept in the design and operation of various ocean structures, including ships, offshore platforms, and coastal defenses. It is the process by which waves exert forces on structures, leading to stress and strain in the structure. This interaction can cause fatigue and failure in structures, which can have serious implications for safety and reliability.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles that govern wave-structure interaction. We will discuss the mathematical models used to describe wave-structure interaction, including the wave-structure interaction matrix and the wave-structure interaction tensor. We will also delve into the practical implications of wave-structure interaction, discussing how it can be used to predict the behavior of structures in wave fields.

We will also discuss the role of wave-structure interaction in the design of structures. This includes the use of wave-structure interaction models in the design process, and the consideration of wave-structure interaction in the design of structures to ensure their safety and reliability.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of wave-structure interaction, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools to understand and predict the behavior of structures in wave fields. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of ocean engineering, this chapter will provide you with a solid foundation in wave-structure interaction.




#### 5.2b Measurement of Wave Momentum Density

The measurement of wave momentum density is a critical aspect of understanding wave-structure interaction. It involves the use of various instruments and techniques to measure the wave momentum and the wave area. 

One such instrument is the Deep-ocean Assessment and Reporting of Tsunamis (DART) buoy system. This system is designed to measure the wave momentum density in the deep ocean. It consists of a bottom pressure recorder (BPR) anchored to the bottom of the sea floor, a moored surface buoy connected to the BPR via an acoustic transmission link, and a surface buoy that sends the data by radio to satellites and then to the ground.

The BPR measures the temperature and pressure of the surrounding sea water. The surface buoy then converts this data into an average height of the waves surrounding the buoy. The temperature of the surrounding sea water is important to the calculations because temperature affects the water's density, thus the pressure, and therefore the sea temperature is required to accurately measure the height of the ocean swells.

The wave momentum density can then be calculated using the wave momentum conservation equation, as discussed in the previous section. This equation states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency. The wave vector is the wave propagation direction multiplied by the wave number. The wave number is the inverse of the wavelength. The wave frequency is the inverse of the wave period.

In conclusion, the measurement of wave momentum density is a crucial aspect of understanding wave-structure interaction. It involves the use of various instruments and techniques, and the application of mathematical equations such as the wave momentum conservation equation.

#### 5.2c Wave Momentum Density in Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

The wave momentum density plays a significant role in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. It is a key factor in determining the forces exerted by waves on these systems, which can lead to fatigue and failure if not properly accounted for.

In ships, the wave momentum density is particularly important in the design of the hull. The hull must be able to withstand the forces exerted by waves, which are proportional to the wave momentum density. This is why the hull is typically designed to be stronger in areas where the wave momentum density is highest, such as at the bow and stern.

In offshore energy systems, the wave momentum density is a critical factor in the design of the foundation. The foundation must be able to withstand the forces exerted by waves, which are proportional to the wave momentum density. This is why the foundation is typically designed to be stronger in areas where the wave momentum density is highest, such as at the base of the system.

The wave momentum density can be calculated using the wave momentum conservation equation, as discussed in the previous section. This equation states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency. The wave vector is the wave propagation direction multiplied by the wave number. ## Chapter: Wave Momentum Flux and Ship Motion

### Introduction

The interaction of waves with ships is a complex phenomenon that has been studied extensively in the field of ocean engineering. This chapter, "Wave Momentum Flux and Ship Motion," delves into the intricate dynamics of this interaction, focusing on the concept of wave momentum flux and its relationship with ship motion.

Wave momentum flux, a fundamental concept in fluid dynamics, is the rate at which momentum is transferred by waves. In the context of ship dynamics, it plays a crucial role in determining the forces exerted on a ship by waves. Understanding this concept is essential for predicting the motion of a ship in waves, which is a critical aspect of ship design and operation.

The chapter will explore the mathematical models that describe the wave momentum flux and its interaction with ship motion. These models, expressed in terms of wave amplitude, frequency, and direction, provide a quantitative framework for analyzing the dynamics of wave-ship interaction. The chapter will also discuss the practical implications of these models, such as their use in predicting ship motions and designing ships to withstand wave forces.

In addition, the chapter will delve into the challenges and limitations of current wave momentum flux models. Despite their usefulness, these models are simplifications of a complex physical phenomenon and may not accurately predict ship motion in all conditions. Understanding these limitations is crucial for interpreting the results of wave-ship interaction studies and for future research in this field.

Overall, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to the concept of wave momentum flux and its role in ship motion. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of the principles and applications of wave momentum flux in ship dynamics.




#### 5.2c Wave Momentum Density in Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

The hull of a ship is designed to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and these forces are directly related to the wave momentum density. The foundations of these systems are designed to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and these forces are directly related to the wave momentum density. The wave momentum density also affects the design of the structures themselves, as it influences the forces exerted by waves on the structure.

This equation states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. The wave number is the inverse of the wavelength. The wave frequency is the inverse of the wave period.
