#### 5.3a Definition of Wave Momentum Flux

The wave momentum flux is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is defined as the rate of change of wave momentum per unit area. In other words, it is the force exerted by waves on a unit area of the water surface.

The wave momentum flux is a vector quantity, with both magnitude and direction. The direction of the wave momentum flux is parallel to the direction of wave propagation, and the magnitude is proportional to the square of the wave amplitude.

The wave momentum flux can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
\mathbf{F} = \frac{\partial \mathbf{p}}{\partial A}
$$

where $\mathbf{F}$ is the wave momentum flux, $\mathbf{p}$ is the wave momentum, and $A$ is the area of the water surface.

The wave momentum flux is a crucial parameter in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. It is a key factor in determining the forces exerted by waves on these systems, and thus affects their stability and safety.

In ships, the wave momentum flux is a critical parameter in the design of hulls and decks. The hull of a ship is designed to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and these forces are directly related to the wave momentum flux. The wave momentum flux also affects the design of decks, as it influences the forces exerted by waves on the deck structure.

In offshore energy systems, the wave momentum flux is a key factor in the design of foundations and structures. The foundations of these systems are designed to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and these forces are directly related to the wave momentum flux. The wave momentum flux also affects the design of the structures themselves, as it influences the forces exerted by waves on the structure.

The wave momentum flux can be calculated using the wave momentum conservation equation, as discussed in the previous section. This equation states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency. The wave vector is the wave propagation direction multiplied by the wave number. The wave number is the inverse of the wavelength. The wave frequency is the inverse of the wave period.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of wave momentum flux on the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.3b Calculation of Wave Momentum Flux

The calculation of wave momentum flux involves the application of the wave momentum conservation equation. This equation states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the negative divergence of the wave force. The wave force is the product of the wave amplitude, the wave vector, and the wave frequency. The wave vector is the wave propagation direction multiplied by the wave number. The wave number is the inverse of the wavelength. The wave frequency is the inverse of the wave period.

The wave momentum conservation equation can be expressed mathematically as:

$$
\frac{\partial \mathbf{p}}{\partial t} = -\nabla \cdot \mathbf{F}
$$

where $\mathbf{p}$ is the wave momentum, $\mathbf{F}$ is the wave force, and $t$ is time.

The wave force $\mathbf{F}$ can be calculated using the wave amplitude $A$, the wave vector $k$, and the wave frequency $\omega$:

$$
\mathbf{F} = A^2 k \omega
$$

The wave vector $k$ is given by the wave propagation direction $n$ and the wave number $k$:

$$
k = n k
$$

The wave number $k$ is the inverse of the wavelength $\lambda$:

$$
k = \frac{2\pi}{\lambda}
$$

The wave frequency $\omega$ is the inverse of the wave period $T$:

$$
\omega = \frac{2\pi}{T}
$$

By substituting these expressions into the wave momentum conservation equation, we can obtain the wave momentum flux $\mathbf{F}$:

$$
\mathbf{F} = \frac{\partial \mathbf{p}}{\partial t} = -\nabla \cdot \left(A^2 k \omega\right)
$$

This equation allows us to calculate the wave momentum flux at any point in the water surface. The wave momentum flux is a crucial parameter in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems, as it is directly related to the forces exerted by waves on these systems.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of wave momentum flux on the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 5.3c Wave Momentum Flux in Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

The wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. It is a key factor in determining the forces exerted by waves on these systems, and thus affects their stability and safety.

In ships, the wave momentum flux is a critical parameter in the design of hulls and decks. The hull of a ship is designed to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and these forces are directly related to the wave momentum flux. The wave momentum flux also affects the design of decks, as it influences the forces exerted by waves on the deck structure.

In offshore energy systems, the wave momentum flux is a key factor in the design of foundations and structures. The foundations of these systems are designed to withstand the forces exerted by waves, and these forces are directly related to the wave momentum flux. The wave momentum flux also affects the design of the structures themselves, as it influences the forces exerted by waves on the structure.

The wave momentum flux can be calculated using the wave momentum conservation equation, as discussed in the previous section. This equation allows us to calculate the wave momentum flux at any point in the water surface. By integrating the wave momentum flux over the entire water surface, we can obtain the total wave momentum flux. This total wave momentum flux is a measure of the total force exerted by waves on the water surface.

In the next section, we will discuss the effects of wave momentum flux on the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 5.3b Measurement of Wave Momentum Flux

The measurement of wave momentum flux is a critical aspect of understanding and predicting the behavior of waves in the ocean. This section will discuss the various methods and technologies used to measure wave momentum flux.

##### QuikSCAT

One of the most widely used technologies for measuring wave momentum flux is the QuikSCAT (Quick Scatterometer). This active microwave radar system emits pulses of low-power microwave radiation and measures the power reflected back to its receiving antenna from the wind-roughened sea surface. The backscatter measurements are obtained at fixed incidence angles of 46° and 54°, providing up to four views of each region of the surface at different incidence angles.

The QuikSCAT system uses a dual-beam, conically scanning antenna that samples the full range of azimuth angles during each antenna revolution. This allows for a spatial resolution of about 25 km, with a higher resolution of 12.5 km achievable through special processing, and an even higher resolution of 5 km for limited regions and special cases.

The backscatter measurements are calibrated to the wind speed and direction of the wind at a reference height of 10 meters above the sea surface. This calibration allows for the estimation of wave momentum flux, which is a key parameter in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

##### Other Methods

While the QuikSCAT system is one of the most widely used technologies for measuring wave momentum flux, there are other methods and technologies available. These include the use of wave riders, which are small, buoyant devices that ride on the surface of the waves and measure their properties. Wave riders can provide detailed information about the wave field, including the wave momentum flux.

Another method is the use of wave radar, which uses radar technology to measure the properties of waves. Wave radar can provide information about the wave momentum flux, as well as other properties such as wave height and direction.

In addition to these technologies, there are also various numerical models and simulations that can be used to estimate wave momentum flux. These models and simulations use mathematical equations and computer algorithms to simulate the behavior of waves in the ocean, and can provide valuable insights into the wave momentum flux.

In conclusion, the measurement of wave momentum flux is a critical aspect of understanding and predicting the behavior of waves in the ocean. Various technologies and methods are available for this purpose, each with its own advantages and limitations. The choice of method depends on the specific requirements and constraints of the application.

#### 5.3c Wave Momentum Flux in Ship Design

The design of ships is a complex process that involves a multitude of factors, including the interaction of waves with the ship. The wave momentum flux, as discussed in the previous sections, plays a crucial role in this interaction. In this section, we will explore how the wave momentum flux is considered in the design of ships, particularly in the context of wave-induced motions and ship motions.

##### Wave-Induced Motions

Wave-induced motions are a significant factor in ship design. These motions, which include heave, pitch, and roll, are primarily caused by the interaction of waves with the ship. The wave momentum flux is a key parameter in predicting these motions.

The wave momentum flux can be used to calculate the wave-induced forces on the ship. These forces, in turn, can be used to determine the wave-induced motions of the ship. This is particularly important in the design of ships that operate in rough seas, where wave-induced motions can significantly affect the ship's stability and safety.

##### Ship Motions

Ship motions, including heave, pitch, and roll, are also influenced by the wave momentum flux. The wave momentum flux can be used to calculate the wave-induced forces on the ship, which can then be used to determine the ship's motions.

In the design of ships, it is crucial to ensure that the ship's motions are within acceptable limits. Excessive motions can lead to instability and safety issues, while insufficient motions can result in poor performance and efficiency. The wave momentum flux plays a key role in achieving this balance.

##### Wave-Induced Forces

The wave-induced forces on a ship are a function of the wave momentum flux. These forces can be calculated using the wave momentum conservation equation, which states that the rate of change of wave momentum is equal to the sum of the forces acting on the wave.

In the context of ship design, the wave-induced forces are particularly important. They can be used to determine the ship's motions, as well as the stresses and strains on the ship's structure. This information is crucial in the design and analysis of ships, and the wave momentum flux plays a key role in providing this information.

In conclusion, the wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the design of ships. It is used to predict wave-induced motions, calculate wave-induced forces, and ensure that the ship's motions are within acceptable limits. The QuikSCAT system, as discussed in the previous section, is one of the technologies used to measure the wave momentum flux. Other methods and technologies are also available, each with its own advantages and limitations.

### Conclusion

## Chapter: Wave-Structure Interaction

### Introduction

The interaction of waves with structures is a fundamental aspect of ocean engineering. This chapter, "Wave-Structure Interaction," delves into the complex dynamics of this interaction, exploring the principles that govern the behavior of waves as they encounter and interact with various structures.

The ocean is a dynamic environment, with waves constantly interacting with the structures that are built within it. These structures, which can range from small offshore platforms to large ships, are designed to withstand these interactions. However, understanding and predicting these interactions is crucial for ensuring the safety and efficiency of these structures.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles that govern wave-structure interaction. We will delve into the mathematical models that describe these interactions, and discuss how these models can be used to predict the behavior of waves as they interact with different types of structures.

We will also discuss the practical implications of wave-structure interaction. This includes the design and analysis of structures, as well as the prediction of wave-induced forces and motions. We will also explore the role of wave-structure interaction in the design of offshore energy systems, such as wave energy converters.

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive guide to wave-structure interaction, providing the knowledge and tools necessary to understand and predict these complex interactions. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a practicing engineer, we hope that this chapter will enhance your understanding of wave-structure interaction and its importance in ocean engineering.




#### 5.3c Effects of Wave Momentum Flux on Wave Dynamics

The wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of waves in the ocean. It is a key factor in the transfer of energy and momentum between the waves and the surrounding medium, which can significantly impact the behavior of the waves.

##### Wave Propagation

The wave momentum flux affects the propagation of waves in the ocean. The wave momentum flux is a measure of the force exerted by the waves on the surrounding medium. This force can either be restorative or dissipative, depending on the nature of the medium.

In a dissipative medium, such as water, the wave momentum flux is dissipative, meaning it tends to decrease the amplitude of the waves. This is due to the frictional forces between the waves and the water molecules, which cause the waves to lose energy and momentum. This dissipation of wave momentum flux can lead to the eventual dissipation of the waves, especially in deep water where the wave amplitude decreases exponentially with depth.

In a restorative medium, such as a fluid-filled elastic medium, the wave momentum flux is restorative, meaning it tends to increase the amplitude of the waves. This is due to the elastic forces in the medium, which can restore the waves to their original shape after they have been distorted by the waves. This restorative effect can lead to the amplification of the waves, especially in shallow water where the wave amplitude increases with depth.

##### Wave Interactions

The wave momentum flux also plays a crucial role in wave interactions. Wave interactions occur when two or more waves interact with each other, leading to the formation of new waves or the modification of the original waves. The wave momentum flux is a key factor in these interactions, as it determines the force exerted by the waves on each other.

For example, in the case of wave reflection, the wave momentum flux of the incident wave is reflected back into the medium as a reflected wave. The angle of reflection is determined by the law of reflection, which states that the angle of incidence is equal to the angle of reflection. The wave momentum flux of the reflected wave is then transferred back into the medium, contributing to the overall wave momentum flux.

In the case of wave refraction, the wave momentum flux of the incident wave is transferred into the medium as a refracted wave. The angle of refraction is determined by Snell's law, which states that the ratio of the sine of the angle of incidence to the sine of the angle of refraction is equal to the inverse ratio of the refractive indices of the two media. The wave momentum flux of the refracted wave is then transferred into the medium, contributing to the overall wave momentum flux.

##### Wave Breaking

The wave momentum flux also plays a crucial role in wave breaking. Wave breaking occurs when the wave amplitude becomes too large, leading to the formation of turbulent water and the dissipation of the wave energy. The wave momentum flux is a key factor in this process, as it determines the force exerted by the waves on the surrounding medium.

In deep water, where the wave amplitude decreases exponentially with depth, wave breaking is less likely to occur. However, in shallow water, where the wave amplitude increases with depth, wave breaking can occur more easily. The wave momentum flux can play a crucial role in this process, as it can provide the necessary force to break the waves.

In conclusion, the wave momentum flux plays a crucial role in the dynamics of waves in the ocean. It affects the propagation of waves, wave interactions, and wave breaking, and is a key factor in the transfer of energy and momentum between the waves and the surrounding medium. Understanding the effects of wave momentum flux on wave dynamics is crucial for predicting the behavior of waves in the ocean.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of wave momentum flux and its importance in understanding the interaction between ocean waves and ships or offshore energy systems. We have learned that wave momentum flux is a crucial parameter in predicting the forces exerted by waves on these systems, and it is influenced by various factors such as wave height, wave direction, and the properties of the system itself.

We have also discussed the different methods for calculating wave momentum flux, including the use of spectral models and the wave energy method. These methods provide a more accurate and comprehensive understanding of wave momentum flux, taking into account the complex nature of ocean waves.

Furthermore, we have examined the practical applications of wave momentum flux in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. By understanding wave momentum flux, engineers and researchers can make informed decisions about the design and placement of these systems, leading to improved performance and safety.

In conclusion, wave momentum flux is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is a crucial parameter in predicting the forces exerted by waves on these systems and plays a significant role in their design and operation. By understanding wave momentum flux, we can better understand and mitigate the effects of ocean waves on these systems.

### Section: 6.1 Wavemaker Types:

Wavemakers are essential tools in the study of ocean waves, allowing us to generate and control waves in a controlled environment. In this section, we will explore the different types of wavemakers and their respective advantages and limitations.

#### 6.1a Different Types of Wavemakers

There are several types of wavemakers, each with its own unique characteristics and applications. Some of the most commonly used types of wavemakers include the Taylor wavemaker, the Michell's theory wavemaker, and the Taylor-Michell wavemaker.

The Taylor wavemaker, also known as the Taylor's theory wavemaker, is a type of wavemaker that is commonly used in laboratory experiments to study wave propagation and interaction with different structures. It is based on the principles of Taylor's theory, which describes the motion of a fluid in a circular container. The Taylor wavemaker consists of a circular container filled with fluid, with a rotating paddle at the bottom. The paddle rotates at a constant speed, creating a circular motion of the fluid. This motion is then amplified by the container, resulting in the generation of waves. The Taylor wavemaker is particularly useful for studying the interaction of waves with circular structures, such as offshore oil platforms.

The Michell's theory wavemaker, also known as the Michell's theory wavemaker, is another commonly used type of wavemaker. It is based on the principles of Michell's theory, which describes the motion of a fluid in a rectangular container. The Michell's theory wavemaker consists of a rectangular container filled with fluid, with a reciprocating paddle at the bottom. The paddle moves back and forth at a constant speed, creating a linear motion of the fluid. This motion is then amplified by the container, resulting in the generation of waves. The Michell's theory wavemaker is particularly useful for studying the interaction of waves with rectangular structures, such as ships.

The Taylor-Michell wavemaker, also known as the Taylor-Michell's theory wavemaker, combines the principles of both Taylor's theory and Michell's theory. It is a more advanced type of wavemaker that is used for more complex studies of wave interaction with different structures. The Taylor-Michell wavemaker consists of a circular container filled with fluid, with a rotating paddle at the bottom and a reciprocating paddle at the top. The paddles move at a constant speed, creating a combination of circular and linear motions of the fluid. This results in the generation of more complex waves, making it a valuable tool for studying the interaction of waves with various structures.

Each type of wavemaker has its own advantages and limitations, and the choice of which one to use depends on the specific study or experiment being conducted. In the next section, we will explore the mathematical models used to describe wavemaker motion and the resulting wave patterns.

#### Exercise 1
Consider a Taylor wavemaker with a rotating paddle at the bottom of a circular container. If the paddle rotates at a constant speed of 10 revolutions per minute, what is the resulting wave frequency? Use the equation $f = \frac{1}{2\pi} \sqrt{\frac{g}{r}}$, where $f$ is the wave frequency, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $r$ is the radius of the container.

#### Exercise 2
A Michell's theory wavemaker is used to study the interaction of waves with a rectangular structure. If the paddle moves back and forth at a constant speed of 5 cm/s, what is the resulting wave speed? Use the equation $c = \sqrt{\frac{g}{k}}$, where $c$ is the wave speed, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $k$ is the wave number.

Consider a Taylor-Michell wavemaker, which combines the principles of both Taylor and Michell's theories. If the wavemaker is used to study the interaction of waves with a cylindrical structure, what is the resulting wave frequency? Use the equation $f = \frac{1}{2\pi} \sqrt{\frac{g}{r}}$, where $f$ is the wave frequency, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, and $r$ is the radius of the structure.

## Chapter 7: Wave Propagation:




### Subsection: 6.1c Selection Criteria for Wavemakers

When selecting a wavemaker for a specific application, there are several criteria that should be considered. These criteria include the type of wavemaker, the size and shape of the container, and the desired wave characteristics.

#### Type of Wavemaker

The type of wavemaker is a crucial factor in selecting the appropriate wavemaker for a specific application. As discussed in the previous section, there are several types of wavemakers, each with its own advantages and limitations. For example, a Taylor wavemaker may be more suitable for studying the interaction of waves with a cylindrical structure, while a Michell's theory wavemaker may be more suitable for studying the interaction of waves with a rectangular structure.

#### Size and Shape of the Container

The size and shape of the container also play a significant role in selecting a wavemaker. The size of the container will determine the size of the waves that can be generated, while the shape of the container will determine the type of waves that can be generated. For example, a circular container may be more suitable for generating circular waves, while a rectangular container may be more suitable for generating rectangular waves.

#### Desired Wave Characteristics

The desired wave characteristics, such as frequency and amplitude, should also be considered when selecting a wavemaker. The frequency of the waves can be controlled by adjusting the speed of the paddle, while the amplitude of the waves can be controlled by adjusting the size of the paddle. Additionally, the shape of the paddle can also affect the characteristics of the waves, such as the steepness of the wave front.

In conclusion, the selection of a wavemaker should be based on the specific application and the desired wave characteristics. By considering the type of wavemaker, the size and shape of the container, and the desired wave characteristics, an appropriate wavemaker can be selected for a given application. 





### Subsection: 6.2a Definition of Linear Wavemaker Theory

Linear wavemaker theory is a mathematical model used to describe the generation of waves in a fluid medium. It is based on the principles of linearization, which assumes that the wave amplitude is small compared to the wavelength. This allows us to simplify the equations of motion and make analytical calculations.

The linear wavemaker theory is based on the following assumptions:

1. The fluid medium is homogeneous and incompressible.
2. The wave amplitude is small compared to the wavelength.
3. The wave propagation is in a horizontal plane.
4. The wavemaker is a rigid body with a constant shape.
5. The wavemaker is moving with a constant velocity.

Under these assumptions, the equations of motion can be linearized and solved analytically. The resulting theory provides a fundamental understanding of wave generation and propagation, and serves as a basis for more advanced theories and models.

The linear wavemaker theory is particularly useful for studying the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems. By understanding the generation and propagation of waves, we can design more efficient ships and offshore structures, and predict the behavior of waves in the ocean.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the linear wavemaker theory, exploring its mathematical foundations, applications, and limitations. We will also discuss the concept of wavemaker strength, which is a key parameter in the theory.

#### 6.2a.1 Wavemaker Strength

The wavemaker strength, denoted by $K$, is a dimensionless parameter that characterizes the efficiency of a wavemaker. It is defined as the ratio of the wave amplitude to the wavemaker velocity, and is given by the equation:

$$
K = \frac{A}{U}
$$

where $A$ is the wave amplitude and $U$ is the wavemaker velocity. The wavemaker strength is a critical parameter in the linear wavemaker theory, as it determines the wave amplitude and the wave propagation characteristics.

The wavemaker strength is a measure of the wavemaker's ability to generate waves. A higher wavemaker strength indicates a more efficient wavemaker, capable of generating larger waves with a given velocity. Conversely, a lower wavemaker strength indicates a less efficient wavemaker, capable of generating smaller waves with a given velocity.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker efficiency, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.2 Wavemaker Efficiency

Wavemaker efficiency, denoted by $\eta$, is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory. It is defined as the ratio of the wave energy generated by the wavemaker to the wave energy that would be generated by an ideal wavemaker. The wavemaker efficiency is given by the equation:

$$
\eta = \frac{E_{gen}}{E_{ideal}}
$$

where $E_{gen}$ is the wave energy generated by the wavemaker and $E_{ideal}$ is the wave energy that would be generated by an ideal wavemaker. The wavemaker efficiency is a measure of the wavemaker's performance, and is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers.

The wavemaker efficiency is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to 1. A wavemaker efficiency of 1 indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with the same energy as an ideal wavemaker. Conversely, a wavemaker efficiency of 0 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating very little wave energy.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker power, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.3 Wavemaker Power

Wavemaker power, denoted by $P$, is a measure of the energy input into the wavemaker. It is defined as the product of the wavemaker strength and the wavemaker velocity, and is given by the equation:

$$
P = K \cdot U
$$

where $K$ is the wavemaker strength and $U$ is the wavemaker velocity. The wavemaker power is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker power is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to infinity. A wavemaker power of infinity indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with infinite energy. Conversely, a wavemaker power of 0 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating very little wave energy.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker displacement, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.4 Wavemaker Displacement

Wavemaker displacement, denoted by $D$, is a measure of the wavemaker's motion. It is defined as the product of the wavemaker strength and the wavemaker displacement, and is given by the equation:

$$
D = K \cdot D
$$

where $K$ is the wavemaker strength and $D$ is the wavemaker displacement. The wavemaker displacement is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker displacement is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to infinity. A wavemaker displacement of infinity indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with infinite amplitude. Conversely, a wavemaker displacement of 0 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating very little wave amplitude.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker damping, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.5 Wavemaker Damping

Wavemaker damping, denoted by $D$, is a measure of the wavemaker's ability to dissipate wave energy. It is defined as the ratio of the wave energy dissipated by the wavemaker to the wave energy generated by the wavemaker, and is given by the equation:

$$
D = \frac{E_{diss}}{E_{gen}}
$$

where $E_{diss}$ is the wave energy dissipated by the wavemaker and $E_{gen}$ is the wave energy generated by the wavemaker. The wavemaker damping is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker damping is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to 1. A wavemaker damping of 0 indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with no energy dissipation. Conversely, a wavemaker damping of 1 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating waves with maximum energy dissipation.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker frequency, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.6 Wavemaker Frequency

Wavemaker frequency, denoted by $f$, is a measure of the wavemaker's oscillation rate. It is defined as the number of oscillations per unit time, and is given by the equation:

$$
f = \frac{1}{T}
$$

where $T$ is the period of the wavemaker's oscillation. The wavemaker frequency is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker frequency is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to infinity. A wavemaker frequency of infinity indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with infinite oscillation rate. Conversely, a wavemaker frequency of 0 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating waves with no oscillation.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker wavelength, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.7 Wavemaker Wavelength

Wavemaker wavelength, denoted by $\lambda$, is a measure of the wavemaker's wave generation length. It is defined as the distance between two consecutive wave crests, and is given by the equation:

$$
\lambda = \frac{2\pi A}{f}
$$

where $A$ is the wave amplitude and $f$ is the wave frequency. The wavemaker wavelength is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker wavelength is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to infinity. A wavemaker wavelength of infinity indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with infinite length. Conversely, a wavemaker wavelength of 0 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating waves with no length.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker amplitude, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.8 Wavemaker Amplitude

Wavemaker amplitude, denoted by $A$, is a measure of the wavemaker's wave generation strength. It is defined as the maximum displacement of the wavemaker from its equilibrium position, and is given by the equation:

$$
A = \frac{\lambda}{2\pi} \cdot f
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavemaker wavelength and $f$ is the wave frequency. The wavemaker amplitude is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker amplitude is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to infinity. A wavemaker amplitude of infinity indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with infinite amplitude. Conversely, a wavemaker amplitude of 0 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating waves with no amplitude.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker phase, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.9 Wavemaker Phase

Wavemaker phase, denoted by $\phi$, is a measure of the wavemaker's wave generation phase. It is defined as the angle between the wavemaker's oscillation and the wave crest, and is given by the equation:

$$
\phi = \arctan\left(\frac{\partial_t \theta}{\partial_x \theta}\right)
$$

where $\theta$ is the wave phase. The wavemaker phase is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker phase is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to 360 degrees. A wavemaker phase of 0 indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with a phase of 0 degrees. Conversely, a wavemaker phase of 360 degrees indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating waves with a phase of 360 degrees.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker power, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.10 Wavemaker Power

Wavemaker power, denoted by $P$, is a measure of the wavemaker's wave generation power. It is defined as the product of the wavemaker amplitude and the wavemaker frequency, and is given by the equation:

$$
P = \frac{1}{2} \rho g A^2 f
$$

where $\rho$ is the fluid density, $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity, $A$ is the wave amplitude, and $f$ is the wave frequency. The wavemaker power is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

The wavemaker power is a dimensionless parameter, and it can range from 0 to infinity. A wavemaker power of infinity indicates a perfect wavemaker, capable of generating waves with infinite power. Conversely, a wavemaker power of 0 indicates a poor wavemaker, capable of generating waves with no power.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of wavemaker displacement, which is another important parameter in the linear wavemaker theory.

#### 6.2a.11 Wavemaker Displacement

Wavemaker displacement, denoted by $D$, is a measure of the wavemaker's wave generation displacement. It is defined as the maximum displacement of the wavemaker from its equilibrium position, and is given by the equation:

$$
D = \frac{\lambda}{2\pi} \cdot f
$$

where $\lambda$ is the wavemaker wavelength and $f$ is the wave frequency. The wavemaker displacement is a critical parameter in the design and analysis of wavemakers, as it determines the maximum wave amplitude that can be generated by the wavemaker.

#### 6.2b Linear Wavemaker Equations

The linear wavemaker equations are a set of differential equations that describe the motion of a wavemaker in a fluid medium. These equations are derived from the principles of linearization and the assumptions of the linear wavemaker theory.

The linear wavemaker equations are given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
\frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial t^2} - c^2 \frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial x^2} &= 0 \\
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} &= \frac{1}{c^2} \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t}
\end{align*}
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, and $c$ is the wave speed. These equations describe the propagation of a wave in the $x$ direction with a speed $c$.

The first equation is the wave equation, which describes the propagation of a wave in space and time. The second equation is the dispersion relation, which relates the wave speed to the wave amplitude.

The linear wavemaker equations are used to study the generation and propagation of waves in a fluid medium. They are particularly useful for studying the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 6.2b.1 Wavemaker Strength and the Linear Wavemaker Equations

The wavemaker strength, $K$, plays a crucial role in the linear wavemaker equations. As mentioned earlier, the wavemaker strength is defined as the ratio of the wave amplitude to the wavemaker velocity. It is given by the equation:

$$
K = \frac{A}{U}
$$

where $A$ is the wave amplitude and $U$ is the wavemaker velocity. The wavemaker strength is a dimensionless parameter that characterizes the efficiency of a wavemaker.

The wavemaker strength is related to the linear wavemaker equations through the dispersion relation. As the wavemaker strength increases, the wave speed $c$ decreases, leading to a decrease in the dispersion relation. This results in a decrease in the wave amplitude, which is proportional to the wave speed. Therefore, as the wavemaker strength increases, the wave amplitude decreases, leading to a decrease in the wavemaker efficiency.

In conclusion, the wavemaker strength plays a crucial role in the linear wavemaker equations and the propagation of waves in a fluid medium. It is a key parameter in the study of wave generation and propagation, and is particularly important in the design and analysis of ships and offshore energy systems.

#### 6.2b.2 Nonlinear Wavemaker Equations

While the linear wavemaker equations provide a useful starting point for understanding wave generation and propagation, they are limited in their ability to capture the complex dynamics of real-world systems. In particular, they do not account for the nonlinear effects that can arise in fluid dynamics, such as wave-wave interactions and wave breaking.

The nonlinear wavemaker equations are a set of differential equations that describe the motion of a wavemaker in a fluid medium, taking into account these nonlinear effects. These equations are derived from the principles of nonlinear wave theory and the assumptions of the nonlinear wavemaker theory.

The nonlinear wavemaker equations are given by:

$$
\begin{align*}
\frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial t^2} - c^2 \frac{\partial^2 \eta}{\partial x^2} &= 0 \\
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} &= \frac{1}{c^2} \frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} + \frac{1}{c^2} \frac{\partial}{\partial x} \left( \frac{1}{2} \eta^2 \right)
\end{align*}
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, and $c$ is the wave speed. These equations describe the propagation of a wave in the $x$ direction with a speed $c$, taking into account the nonlinear effects of wave-wave interactions and wave breaking.

The nonlinear wavemaker equations are used to study the generation and propagation of waves in a fluid medium, taking into account the complex dynamics of real-world systems. They are particularly useful for studying the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems, where nonlinear effects can play a crucial role.

#### 6.2b.3 Wavemaker Strength and the Nonlinear Wavemaker Equations

The wavemaker strength, $K$, also plays a crucial role in the nonlinear wavemaker equations. As mentioned earlier, the wavemaker strength is defined as the ratio of the wave amplitude to the wavemaker velocity. It is given by the equation:

$$
K = \frac{A}{U}
$$

where $A$ is the wave amplitude and $U$ is the wavemaker velocity. The wavemaker strength is a dimensionless parameter that characterizes the efficiency of a wavemaker.

In the nonlinear wavemaker equations, the wavemaker strength plays a more complex role. As the wavemaker strength increases, the wave speed $c$ decreases, leading to an increase in the dispersion relation. This results in an increase in the wave amplitude, which is proportional to the wave speed. Therefore, as the wavemaker strength increases, the wave amplitude increases, leading to an increase in the wavemaker efficiency.

However, this increase in wave amplitude can also lead to the onset of nonlinear effects, such as wave-wave interactions and wave breaking. These effects can limit the efficiency of the wavemaker, and the nonlinear wavemaker equations provide a way to account for these effects.

In conclusion, the wavemaker strength plays a crucial role in the nonlinear wavemaker equations, both in terms of the wavemaker efficiency and the onset of nonlinear effects. Understanding the relationship between the wavemaker strength and the nonlinear wavemaker equations is essential for studying the generation and propagation of waves in a fluid medium.




#### 6.2c Applications of Linear Wavemaker Theory

The linear wavemaker theory has a wide range of applications in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. In this section, we will discuss some of these applications in more detail.

##### 6.2c.1 Wavemaker Design and Analysis

The linear wavemaker theory is used in the design and analysis of wavemakers for various applications. For example, in the design of wavemakers for wave energy conversion, the linear wavemaker theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the energy conversion efficiency.

The linear wavemaker theory is also used in the analysis of wavemakers for wave mitigation. In this case, the theory is used to determine the wavemaker strength and shape that can effectively reduce the wave height in a given area.

##### 6.2c.2 Wave Propagation and Interaction

The linear wavemaker theory is also used in the study of wave propagation and interaction. For example, in the study of wave propagation in shallow water, the linear wavemaker theory is used to determine the wave speed and amplitude as a function of the wavemaker strength and shape.

In the study of wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, the linear wavemaker theory is used to determine the wave amplitude and phase shift as a function of the wavemaker strength and shape. This information is crucial for understanding the wave-structure interaction and for designing structures that can withstand the wave loads.

##### 6.2c.3 Wave Energy Conversion

The linear wavemaker theory is used in the design and analysis of wave energy conversion devices. These devices convert the kinetic energy of the waves into electrical or mechanical energy. The linear wavemaker theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the energy conversion efficiency.

##### 6.2c.4 Wave Mitigation

The linear wavemaker theory is used in the design and analysis of wave mitigation devices. These devices are used to reduce the wave height in a given area. The linear wavemaker theory is used to determine the wavemaker strength and shape that can effectively reduce the wave height.

##### 6.2c.5 Wave Propagation in Shallow Water

The linear wavemaker theory is used in the study of wave propagation in shallow water. The theory is used to determine the wave speed and amplitude as a function of the wavemaker strength and shape. This information is crucial for understanding the wave propagation in shallow water and for designing structures that can withstand the wave loads.

##### 6.2c.6 Wave Interaction with Ships and Offshore Energy Systems

The linear wavemaker theory is used in the study of wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. The theory is used to determine the wave amplitude and phase shift as a function of the wavemaker strength and shape. This information is crucial for understanding the wave-structure interaction and for designing structures that can withstand the wave loads.




#### 6.3a Definition of Nonlinear Wavemaker Theory

Nonlinear wavemaker theory is a branch of wavemaker theory that deals with the generation of nonlinear waves. Unlike linear waves, which are governed by the linear wavemaker theory, nonlinear waves are characterized by their nonlinear dependence on the wavemaker strength and shape. This nonlinear dependence leads to a more complex wavemaker behavior, which is the focus of nonlinear wavemaker theory.

The nonlinear wavemaker theory is based on the concept of wavemaker strength, which is defined as the ratio of the wavemaker amplitude to the wavemaker length. The wavemaker strength is a crucial parameter in the generation of nonlinear waves. It determines the amplitude and frequency of the generated waves, as well as their propagation characteristics.

The nonlinear wavemaker theory also considers the wavemaker shape, which is defined as the shape of the wavemaker relative to the incoming wave. The wavemaker shape is another important parameter in the generation of nonlinear waves. It determines the phase shift and group delay of the generated waves, as well as their dispersion characteristics.

The nonlinear wavemaker theory is used in the design and analysis of wavemakers for various applications. For example, in the design of wavemakers for wave energy conversion, the nonlinear wavemaker theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the energy conversion efficiency.

In the study of wave propagation and interaction, the nonlinear wavemaker theory is used to determine the wave amplitude and phase shift as a function of the wavemaker strength and shape. This information is crucial for understanding the wave-structure interaction and for designing structures that can withstand the wave loads.

The nonlinear wavemaker theory is also used in the design and analysis of wave energy conversion devices. These devices convert the kinetic energy of the waves into electrical or mechanical energy. The nonlinear wavemaker theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the energy conversion efficiency.

Finally, the nonlinear wavemaker theory is used in the design and analysis of wave mitigation devices. These devices are used to reduce the wave height and wave energy in a given area. The nonlinear wavemaker theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the wave mitigation efficiency.

#### 6.3b Nonlinear Wavemaker Theory in Wave Generation

The nonlinear wavemaker theory plays a crucial role in the generation of nonlinear waves. The theory is based on the concept of wavemaker strength and wavemaker shape, which are defined as the ratio of the wavemaker amplitude to the wavemaker length and the shape of the wavemaker relative to the incoming wave, respectively.

The wavemaker strength and shape determine the amplitude and frequency of the generated waves, as well as their propagation characteristics. For example, a higher wavemaker strength leads to a larger amplitude and a higher frequency of the generated waves. Similarly, a more pointed wavemaker shape leads to a larger phase shift and a shorter group delay of the generated waves.

The nonlinear wavemaker theory is used in the design and analysis of wavemakers for various applications. For instance, in the design of wavemakers for wave energy conversion, the theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the energy conversion efficiency. This is achieved by adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape to match the desired wave characteristics, such as amplitude, frequency, and phase shift.

In the study of wave propagation and interaction, the nonlinear wavemaker theory is used to determine the wave amplitude and phase shift as a function of the wavemaker strength and shape. This information is crucial for understanding the wave-structure interaction and for designing structures that can withstand the wave loads. For example, by adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape, the wave amplitude and phase shift can be controlled to minimize the wave loads on a structure.

The nonlinear wavemaker theory is also used in the design and analysis of wave energy conversion devices. These devices convert the kinetic energy of the waves into electrical or mechanical energy. The theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the energy conversion efficiency. This is achieved by adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape to match the desired wave characteristics, such as amplitude, frequency, and phase shift.

Finally, the nonlinear wavemaker theory is used in the design and analysis of wave mitigation devices. These devices are used to reduce the wave height and wave energy in a given area. The theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the wave mitigation efficiency. This is achieved by adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape to match the desired wave characteristics, such as amplitude, frequency, and phase shift.

#### 6.3c Applications of Nonlinear Wavemaker Theory

The nonlinear wavemaker theory has a wide range of applications in the field of ocean engineering. It is used in the design and analysis of various structures and systems that interact with waves, such as ships, offshore energy systems, and wave mitigation devices.

One of the key applications of nonlinear wavemaker theory is in the design of ships. The theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to minimize the wave loads on the ship. This is achieved by adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape to match the desired wave characteristics, such as amplitude, frequency, and phase shift. By doing so, the wave loads on the ship can be minimized, leading to a more stable and efficient ship design.

Another important application of nonlinear wavemaker theory is in the design of offshore energy systems. These systems, such as wind turbines and wave energy converters, interact with waves in a nonlinear manner. The nonlinear wavemaker theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the energy conversion efficiency of these systems. This is achieved by adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape to match the desired wave characteristics, such as amplitude, frequency, and phase shift.

The nonlinear wavemaker theory is also used in the design and analysis of wave mitigation devices. These devices are used to reduce the wave height and wave energy in a given area. The theory is used to determine the optimal wavemaker strength and shape to maximize the wave mitigation efficiency. This is achieved by adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape to match the desired wave characteristics, such as amplitude, frequency, and phase shift.

In addition to these applications, the nonlinear wavemaker theory is also used in the study of wave propagation and interaction. It is used to determine the wave amplitude and phase shift as a function of the wavemaker strength and shape. This information is crucial for understanding the wave-structure interaction and for designing structures that can withstand the wave loads.

In conclusion, the nonlinear wavemaker theory plays a crucial role in the field of ocean engineering. It provides a theoretical framework for understanding and controlling the interaction between waves and structures. By adjusting the wavemaker strength and shape, the wave loads on structures can be minimized, leading to more stable and efficient designs. Furthermore, the theory is used in the design and analysis of offshore energy systems and wave mitigation devices, as well as in the study of wave propagation and interaction.

## Chapter 7: Wave Propagation

### Introduction

The propagation of waves is a fundamental concept in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. This chapter, "Wave Propagation," delves into the intricacies of wave propagation, providing a comprehensive understanding of how waves travel across the ocean's surface.

Wave propagation is a complex phenomenon that involves the transfer of energy from one point to another. In the context of ocean waves, this energy can be in the form of kinetic energy, potential energy, or a combination of both. The propagation of these waves is influenced by a variety of factors, including the depth of the water, the wind speed and direction, and the presence of any obstacles.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe wave propagation. These models, often expressed in terms of differential equations, provide a quantitative description of how waves travel across the ocean's surface. We will also discuss the physical interpretations of these models, helping to bridge the gap between abstract mathematical concepts and real-world phenomena.

We will also delve into the practical applications of wave propagation. For instance, understanding wave propagation is crucial for predicting the behavior of ships in the ocean. It also plays a key role in the design and operation of offshore energy systems, such as wind turbines and wave energy converters.

By the end of this chapter, you should have a solid understanding of wave propagation, its mathematical description, and its practical applications. This knowledge will serve as a foundation for the subsequent chapters, where we will delve deeper into the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems.




#### 6.3b Nonlinear Wavemaker Equations

The nonlinear wavemaker equations are a set of partial differential equations that describe the propagation of nonlinear waves. These equations are derived from the basic principles of fluid dynamics and wave theory, and they provide a mathematical framework for understanding the complex behavior of nonlinear waves.

The nonlinear wavemaker equations can be written in the following form:

$$
\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial t} + c\frac{\partial \eta}{\partial x} + \frac{1}{2}\frac{\partial}{\partial x}\left(\eta^2\right) + \frac{1}{6}\frac{\partial^3 \eta}{\partial x^3} = 0
$$

where $\eta$ is the wave amplitude, $t$ is time, $x$ is the spatial coordinate, and $c$ is the wave speed. The first term on the left-hand side represents the temporal evolution of the wave amplitude, the second term represents the advection of the wave amplitude, and the third and fourth terms represent the nonlinear and cubic dispersion effects, respectively.

The nonlinear wavemaker equations are a simplified form of the more general nonlinear Schrödinger equation, which describes the propagation of nonlinear waves in a variety of physical systems. The nonlinear wavemaker equations are particularly useful for studying the generation of nonlinear waves by wavemakers, as they capture the essential nonlinear effects without the complexity of the full nonlinear Schrödinger equation.

The nonlinear wavemaker equations can be used to derive the Manley-Rowe relations, which describe the conservation of wave action in nonlinear wave interactions. These relations are given by:

$$
\frac{d}{dx}\left(A_1A_2^*\right) + \frac{d}{dx}\left(A_2A_3^*\right) + \frac{d}{dx}\left(A_3A_1^*\right) = 0
$$

where $A_1$, $A_2$, and $A_3$ are the amplitudes of the three waves involved in the interaction. These relations are a direct consequence of the nonlinear wavemaker equations and provide a powerful tool for analyzing nonlinear wave interactions.

In the next section, we will discuss the solutions of the nonlinear wavemaker equations and their physical interpretation.

#### 6.3c Applications of Nonlinear Wavemaker Theory

The nonlinear wavemaker theory has a wide range of applications in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. In this section, we will discuss some of these applications, focusing on the use of the nonlinear wavemaker equations and the Manley-Rowe relations.

##### Wave Generation and Propagation

The nonlinear wavemaker equations are particularly useful for studying the generation of nonlinear waves by wavemakers. These equations capture the essential nonlinear effects without the complexity of the full nonlinear Schrödinger equation, making them a valuable tool for understanding the behavior of nonlinear waves.

For example, the nonlinear wavemaker equations can be used to study the generation of rogue waves, which are large, unexpected waves that can pose a significant threat to ships and offshore structures. The nonlinear wavemaker equations can provide insights into the mechanisms behind the formation of rogue waves, and can help in the development of strategies for mitigating their impact.

##### Nonlinear Wave Interactions

The Manley-Rowe relations, which are derived from the nonlinear wavemaker equations, are particularly useful for studying nonlinear wave interactions. These relations describe the conservation of wave action in nonlinear wave interactions, and can provide insights into the behavior of nonlinear waves in a variety of physical systems.

For example, the Manley-Rowe relations can be used to study the interaction of waves with offshore energy systems, such as wind turbines or wave energy converters. By understanding the conservation of wave action, we can better predict the behavior of these systems under different wave conditions, and can design more efficient and robust systems.

##### Nonlinear Wave Phenomena

The nonlinear wavemaker theory can also be used to study a variety of nonlinear wave phenomena, such as solitons, which are localized waves that maintain their shape while propagating, and simultons, which are three-wave solutions of the nonlinear wavemaker equations that consist of three comoving solitons.

These phenomena can have important implications for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. For example, understanding solitons can help in the design of ships that can withstand large waves, and understanding simultons can help in the design of wave energy converters that can efficiently capture the energy of nonlinear waves.

In conclusion, the nonlinear wavemaker theory provides a powerful tool for studying the complex behavior of nonlinear waves. By understanding the nonlinear wavemaker equations and the Manley-Rowe relations, we can gain insights into the generation, propagation, and interaction of nonlinear waves, and can develop more efficient and robust ships and offshore energy systems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the intricate world of wavemaker theory, a fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. We have explored the principles that govern wave generation and propagation, and how these principles apply to the design and operation of wavemakers. 

We have also examined the mathematical models that describe wavemaker behavior, and how these models can be used to predict the behavior of waves in the ocean. These models, while complex, provide a powerful tool for understanding and predicting the behavior of waves, which is crucial for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

In addition, we have discussed the practical applications of wavemaker theory, including the design of wavemakers for wave energy conversion and the prediction of wave behavior in the vicinity of offshore structures. These applications highlight the importance of wavemaker theory in the field of ocean engineering.

In conclusion, wavemaker theory is a complex but essential field of study in the field of ocean engineering. It provides the theoretical foundation for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems, and for the prediction of wave behavior in the ocean. By understanding wavemaker theory, we can design more efficient and effective ships and offshore energy systems, and can predict the behavior of waves in the ocean with greater accuracy.

## Chapter 7: Wave Propagation

### Introduction

The study of wave propagation is a fundamental aspect of understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems. This chapter will delve into the principles and theories that govern the propagation of waves, and how these principles apply to the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

Wave propagation refers to the movement of waves through a medium, in this case, the ocean. It is a complex phenomenon that involves the transfer of energy and momentum from one point to another. The study of wave propagation is crucial in the design of ships and offshore energy systems, as it helps engineers understand how waves interact with these structures and how they can be designed to withstand these interactions.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe wave propagation, including the wave equation and the principles of linear and nonlinear wave propagation. We will also discuss the effects of wave propagation on ships and offshore energy systems, including the generation of waves and the transfer of energy between waves and these systems.

We will also delve into the practical applications of wave propagation, including the design of ships and offshore energy systems to withstand wave propagation, and the prediction of wave propagation for the purposes of navigation and energy production.

This chapter will provide a comprehensive guide to wave propagation, providing the necessary knowledge and tools for understanding and predicting the behavior of waves in the ocean. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of ocean engineering, this chapter will provide you with the knowledge you need to understand and predict wave propagation.




#### 6.3c Applications of Nonlinear Wavemaker Theory

The nonlinear wavemaker theory has a wide range of applications in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. In this section, we will discuss some of these applications, focusing on the use of the multiple-prism dispersion theory and the three-wave equation.

##### Multiple-Prism Dispersion Theory

The multiple-prism dispersion theory, as extended in 1987, provides explicit second-order equations directly applicable to the design of prismatic pulse compressors. This theory is particularly useful in the design of wavemakers for generating nonlinear waves. By understanding the dispersion properties of the wavemaker, engineers can design more efficient and effective wavemakers for various applications.

##### Three-Wave Equation

The three-wave equation, as introduced by Zabusky and Kruskal in 1965, is a fundamental equation in the study of nonlinear waves. It describes the interaction of three waves with different wave vectors and frequencies. The solutions to this equation can be classified into four classes, depending on the values of the interaction coefficients $\eta_j$.

The three-wave equation has been used to study various phenomena, including the generation of solitons, the interaction of waves with different frequencies, and the formation of rogue waves. In the context of ocean waves, the three-wave equation can be used to study the interaction of waves with different frequencies, which is crucial for understanding the propagation of waves in the ocean.

##### Soliton Exchange

One interesting solution to the three-wave equation is the simulton, which consists of three comoving solitons. This solution has been proposed as a possible explanation for the formation of rogue waves, even though deep water does not have a three-wave resonant interaction. The study of soliton exchange can provide valuable insights into the behavior of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems.

In conclusion, the nonlinear wavemaker theory, with its various applications, provides a powerful tool for understanding the complex behavior of ocean waves. By studying the multiple-prism dispersion theory and the three-wave equation, engineers and scientists can gain a deeper understanding of the propagation of waves in the ocean, which is crucial for the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the intricacies of wavemaker theory, a fundamental concept in the study of ocean wave interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. We have explored the principles that govern wave generation and propagation, and how these principles are applied in the design and operation of wavemakers. 

We have also examined the mathematical models that describe wavemaker behavior, including the linear and nonlinear wavemaker equations. These models provide a theoretical framework for understanding the complex dynamics of wavemakers, and they are essential tools for engineers and scientists working in this field.

Finally, we have discussed the practical applications of wavemaker theory, including its use in the design of wave tanks and the study of wave-structure interactions. We have seen how wavemaker theory can be used to predict the behavior of waves in real-world scenarios, and how it can be used to optimize the performance of ships and offshore energy systems.

In conclusion, wavemaker theory is a powerful tool for understanding and predicting the behavior of ocean waves. It is a complex and multifaceted field, but with a solid understanding of the principles and models discussed in this chapter, one can navigate this complexity and apply wavemaker theory to a wide range of practical problems.

## Chapter 7: Wave Propagation

### Introduction

The study of wave propagation is a fundamental aspect of understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems. This chapter will delve into the principles and theories that govern the propagation of waves, and how these principles are applied in the context of ocean waves.

Wave propagation refers to the movement of waves through a medium, in this case, the ocean. It is a complex phenomenon that involves the transfer of energy and momentum from one point to another. The study of wave propagation is crucial in understanding the behavior of waves, their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems, and their impact on the marine environment.

In this chapter, we will explore the mathematical models that describe wave propagation, including the wave equation and the dispersion relation. We will also discuss the effects of wave propagation on ships and offshore energy systems, such as wave-induced forces and motions.

We will also delve into the practical applications of wave propagation, such as the design of offshore structures and the prediction of wave conditions. This chapter will provide a comprehensive understanding of wave propagation, equipping readers with the knowledge and tools to analyze and predict wave behavior in the ocean.

The study of wave propagation is a vast and complex field, but this chapter aims to provide a clear and accessible introduction to the topic. Whether you are a student, a researcher, or a professional in the field of ocean engineering, this chapter will serve as a valuable resource in your understanding of wave propagation.




#### 6.4a Definition of Wavemaker Efficiency

Wavemaker efficiency is a critical concept in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It is defined as the ratio of the power output of the wavemaker to the power input. In other words, it is a measure of how effectively the wavemaker converts the input power into wave energy.

The efficiency of a wavemaker is influenced by several factors, including the design of the wavemaker, the properties of the fluid (in this case, the ocean water), and the frequency of the wave. The efficiency can be calculated using the following equation:

$$
\eta = \frac{P_{out}}{P_{in}}
$$

where $\eta$ is the efficiency, $P_{out}$ is the power output of the wavemaker, and $P_{in}$ is the power input.

The efficiency of a wavemaker is a crucial factor in the design and operation of ships and offshore energy systems. A wavemaker with high efficiency can generate waves with less power input, which can save energy and reduce the cost of operation. Furthermore, a high efficiency wavemaker can generate waves with a specific frequency and amplitude, which is essential for many applications, such as wave energy conversion and wave testing of ships.

In the next sections, we will discuss the factors that influence wavemaker efficiency and how to improve the efficiency of wavemakers.

#### 6.4b Factors Affecting Wavemaker Efficiency

The efficiency of a wavemaker is influenced by several factors. These factors can be broadly categorized into two groups: design factors and environmental factors.

##### Design Factors

The design of the wavemaker plays a crucial role in its efficiency. The design factors include the shape of the wavemaker, the material used, and the method of power input.

The shape of the wavemaker is particularly important. The shape determines the frequency and amplitude of the waves that the wavemaker can generate. A well-designed wavemaker can generate waves with a specific frequency and amplitude, which can improve the efficiency of the wavemaker.

The material used in the construction of the wavemaker also affects its efficiency. The material should be strong enough to withstand the forces exerted by the waves and the fluid. It should also have good electrical conductivity to facilitate the transfer of power.

The method of power input is another important factor. The power input can be through an electrical connection or through a mechanical connection. The efficiency of the wavemaker can be improved by using a method that minimizes power loss during the transfer of power.

##### Environmental Factors

The properties of the fluid (in this case, the ocean water) and the frequency of the wave also affect the efficiency of the wavemaker.

The properties of the fluid include its density, viscosity, and surface tension. These properties can affect the propagation of the waves and the efficiency of the wavemaker. For example, a higher density of the fluid can increase the resistance to the propagation of the waves, which can decrease the efficiency of the wavemaker.

The frequency of the wave is another important factor. The efficiency of the wavemaker can be improved by operating at a frequency that matches the natural frequency of the wavemaker. This can be achieved by adjusting the design of the wavemaker or by using a frequency controller.

In the next section, we will discuss how to improve the efficiency of wavemakers by optimizing these factors.

#### 6.4c Improving Wavemaker Efficiency

Improving the efficiency of a wavemaker involves optimizing the design factors and the environmental factors. This can be achieved through careful design and selection of materials, as well as through the use of advanced technologies.

##### Design Optimization

The design of the wavemaker can be optimized to improve its efficiency. This can be achieved by adjusting the shape of the wavemaker, the material used, and the method of power input.

The shape of the wavemaker can be optimized to generate waves with a specific frequency and amplitude. This can be achieved by using advanced mathematical models and simulations to predict the behavior of the waves. The shape can also be adjusted to minimize power loss during the propagation of the waves.

The material used in the construction of the wavemaker can be optimized to withstand the forces exerted by the waves and the fluid. This can be achieved by selecting a material with good strength and electrical conductivity. The material can also be optimized to minimize power loss during the transfer of power.

The method of power input can be optimized to minimize power loss during the transfer of power. This can be achieved by using advanced power transfer technologies, such as wireless power transfer or power transfer through optical fibers.

##### Environmental Optimization

The environmental factors can be optimized to improve the efficiency of the wavemaker. This can be achieved by adjusting the properties of the fluid and the frequency of the wave.

The properties of the fluid can be optimized by adjusting the density, viscosity, and surface tension. This can be achieved by using advanced fluid management technologies, such as fluid injection or fluid manipulation.

The frequency of the wave can be optimized by adjusting the design of the wavemaker or by using a frequency controller. This can be achieved by using advanced wave generation technologies, such as wave shaping or wave steering.

In conclusion, improving the efficiency of a wavemaker involves optimizing the design factors and the environmental factors. This can be achieved through careful design and selection of materials, as well as through the use of advanced technologies. By optimizing these factors, it is possible to achieve high efficiency in wave generation, which can have significant benefits for ships and offshore energy systems.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have delved into the intricacies of wavemaker theory, a critical component in understanding the interaction of waves with ships and offshore energy systems. We have explored the fundamental principles that govern wave generation and propagation, and how these principles are applied in the design and operation of wavemakers. 

We have also examined the mathematical models that describe wavemaker behavior, including the linear and nonlinear wavemaker theories. These models provide a quantitative framework for understanding the complex dynamics of wave generation and propagation, and are essential tools in the design and analysis of wavemakers.

Furthermore, we have discussed the practical applications of wavemaker theory in the field of ocean engineering. We have seen how wavemakers are used in the testing and validation of ship designs, as well as in the study of wave-structure interactions in offshore energy systems.

In conclusion, wavemaker theory is a powerful tool in the study of ocean waves and their interaction with ships and offshore energy systems. It provides a deep understanding of wave generation and propagation, and is indispensable in the design and analysis of wavemakers.
